The offer, which stunned Wall Street, is a sign that the consolidation in health care is far from over.   American General made a $2.6 billion bid to acquire Unitrin, a financial services holding company. There was no reaction from Unitrin, which had rejected a previous offer by the insurer.   Thrifty Rent-a-Car System and Ryder Consumer Truck Rental reached an agreement that permits the two companies to use each other's rental services in the United States and Canada.   ICN Pharmaceuticals plans to merge with its three affiliates, creating a company with lower overall administrative costs.   International   South Africa is still awaiting a surge of foreign investment 10 months after Nelson Mandela appealed for businesses to return. The new government is counting on foreign capital to fuel growth and jobs, but investors are worried about high labor costs, trade barriers and the Government's commitment to capitalism.   GATT took a step toward congressional approval. But the key Senate committee that acted on the world trade accord created a problem for the Clinton administration by rejecting its request for a renewal of so-called fast-track authority.   Mexico accused the U.S. steel industry of dumping -- selling products abroad at unfairly low prices. Mexico imposed tariffs on two types of United States steel imports.   The Economy   Sales of new homes fell 14.1 percent in June, the government reported. Analysts said higher interest rates were putting a crimp in housing, an important segment of the economy.   Consumer borrowing and corporate bank loans are rising, suggesting that there is still optimism and growth in the economy. Many economists say that the increase reflects an economy that is healthy today, whatever the future might hold.   Nearly $1 billion in shipbuilding contracts was announced. The government contracts will benefit shipyards in California, Louisiana, Mississippi and Virginia.   Markets   Three recent technical breakdowns at Nasdaq have tarnished the exchange's image as as a technological leader and has prompted concern from securities traders and lawmakers.   Prices of Treasury securities were mixed. Short-term securities rose, while the price of the long bond fell.   Blue-chip stocks slipped in listless trading.   The London stock market led rallies by European exchanges. China's stock market gave back part of Monday's gains.   The dollar rose against the yen as the outlook improved for trade relations between the United States and Japan.   Crude oil prices tumbled amid speculation that political unrest in Nigeria would ease. Gold prices fell sharply.   Business Technology   Olivetti has recast itself as an information technology company and appears to be on its way toward achieving a turnaround. The troubled Italian giant has expanded beyond computers in recent years to focus on designing, installing and maintaining computer networks for big corporate European customers.   Columns   A power outage was not the only embarrassment for Nasdaq on Monday. Nasdaq tried to halt trading in the shares of Newbridge Networks, but somehow failed. Sometimes, it appears, Nasdaq cannot make its systems work, and other times it cannot prevent them from functioning.   Is cyberspace ready for advertising? A year ago, that seemed to be the case when Bobby Rahal,who bore the torch for Honda's debut in the PPG Indy Car World Series, ended his season by switching to Mercedes-Benz. Enter Steve Horne,owner of Tasman Motorsports, who this season helped Honda save face by joining forces with Robert Clarke,general manager of Honda Performance Development, to put a competitive package on the track for drivers Scott Goodyear and rookie Andre Ribeiro. ``What we have now is a totally different engine, with an alloy block and revised induction system,'' Clarke said. ``Most of the performance gains have been made in the induction area.'' While they ran an old HRX engine last season and in the first five races this season, Honda engineers developed a new aluminum block engine, the HRH. ``I think the opportunity to work with Honda is a once-in-a-lifetime proposition,'' said Horne. ``When the opportunity came up, there was no question in my mind what we wanted to do, because there was no downside for us.'' Even by marrying the engine with a Reynard chassis and Firestone tires? ``I didn't feel like we were rolling the dice with Honda and Firestone,'' Horne said. ``I think I looked at the facts. And the facts are that Honda had a difficult year last year, but if you look at their previous history in racing (specifically in Formula One, where Honda developed a dominant engine for Team McLaren), you'd have to be blind not to recognize their record of success over the years. I didn't see any dice involved in that.'' ... Scott Sharp of East Norwalk, Conn., who made his Indy 500 debut last year with PacWest Racing, landed a ride with A.J. Foyt and will drive a year-old Lola-Ford Cosworth. During Friday's practice, Sharp went 224.003 m.p.h., the fastest tour he's ever made at the Brickyard. ``That was a lot faster than I thought we had run,'' said Sharp, who qualified 17th with a four-lap average of 222.091 m.p.h, a year ago. ``A.J. is just fantastic, honestly. Every change he's made has been a good change. You don't usually get that. You usually get a couple of good ones then one in the wrong direction. But he's just really helped me out. We're in a '94 car, so I'll be happy with any spot that will get me in securely.'' </P> "	"nyt_eng_199505.gz"

Unfortunately, as I noted in Allegheny, ``even the development of articulable standards and guidelines has not always resulted in agreement among the Members of this Court on the results in individual cases.'' 492 U. S., at 623. Today, JUSTICE STEVENS reaches a different conclusion regarding whether the Board's decision to allow respondents' display on Capitol Square constituted an impermissible endorsement of the cross' religious message. Top Stories  SMITH-NEW-COURT-MERRILL (London) -- Smith New Court Plc agreed to a takeover by Merrill Lynch for 526.3 million pounds, or $836 million, ending a two-week bidding contest for the UK's No. 1 independent stockbroker.  JAPAN-AIR-CARGO (Los Angeles) -- The U.S. and Japan agreed last night to broaden access to air-cargo routes through both countries. Negotiators are meeting this morning to iron out details.  BLINDER-INTERVIEW (Washington) -- Alan Blinder, vice chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, said he's ``open-minded'' about the need for another cut in short-term interest rates to stimulate the economy. ``The probability of a recession is lower in mid-July than it was in the middle of June,'' Blinder told the Bloomberg Forum. ``But that doesn't mean the risk is zero.''  SPRINT-QUALCOMM (Kansas City, Missouri) -- Sprint Corp.'s alliance with three cable-television companies picks Qualcomm Inc.'s digital technology for its nationwide wireless network, a move that could mean millions of dollars for Qualcomm.  ADM-MULRONEY (Decatur, Illinois) -- Former Canadian Prime Minister Brian Mulroney says his committee of outside Archer Daniels Midland Co. directors will act objectively and openly in response to price-fixing allegations.  BANK-OF-BOSTON-STEPANIAN (Boston) -- With reports that Bank of Boston Corp. may merge with Corestates Financial Corp., it appears Chairman Ira Stepanian either scaled back his demands in finding a merger partner or found a partner willing to meet them.  WESTINGHOUSE-CBS (New York) -- Westinghouse Electric Corp. and CBS Inc. officials are negotiating terms of a formal takeover bid by Westinghouse today, people familiar with the talks said.  WESTINGHOUSE-CBS-FUTURE (New York) -- On David Letterman's ``Late Show,'' Mary Tyler Moore lamented that her new CBS drama, ``New York News,'' would have to compete against NBC's hit series, ``Seinfeld,'' on Thursday evenings this fall. Moore shrugged her shoulders and said, ``We'll just have to do our best,'' So, it appears, will Westinghouse Electric Co. The Pittsburgh giant is in talks to buy the nation's third-rated network.  MICROSOFT-JUSTICE (Washington) -- The Justice Department withdrew a disputed demand for information in its antitrust probe of Microsoft Corp., saying the fast-approaching deadline for release of Windows 95 doesn't leave time for a procedural fight. The government said it's continuing its probe of possible antitrust violations in Microsoft's plan to use its ballyhooed Windows 95 operating system as a launching pad into computer information services.  What The Markets Are Saying  US-BONDS (New York) -- U.S. bonds fell and yields rose to the highest in about two months as investors bet the economy's growth won't slow enough to renew a recent rally.  COMMODITIES (New York) -- Commodities were higher, with coffee prices jumping amid perceptions a plan by producers to withhold exports is working to lower available supply.  US-STOCKS (New York) -- U.S. stocks were mixed as the outlook for technology companies varied. Apple Computer Inc.'s fiscal third-quarter were beneath last year's, and less than analysts' consensus forecast. LSI Logic Corp. said second-quarter net income would be above analysts' forecast of 38 cents.  DOLLAR (New York) -- The dollar rose against the yen as the conviction grew that the Bank of Japan is willing to buy dollars aggressively to drive the U.S. currency higher.  Company News  FEDEX-JAPAN (Memphis, Tennessee) -- Federal Express Corp.'s stock climbed on news that it will begin operating its Subic Bay hub in earlier September, allowing it to increase service throughout Asia.  EARNS-USAIR (Arlington, Virginia) -- USAir Group Inc. says second-quarter profit increased more than eight-fold as the airline recorded the largest quarterly revenue in its history.  Washington Report  SPENDING-CUTS (Washington) -- A bill to cut current spending by $16.4 billion is expected to win final congressional approval. It would be a small, yet symbolic, first step in the Republicans' quest to balance the budget by the 2002.  US-ECONOMY (Washington) -- On Monday, the White House and Congress are likely to the kind of news they like: that the government posted a budget surplus in June as quarterly tax returns streamed into the Treasury's coffers. However, analysts say, the outlook for the months and years ahead isn't quite as upbeat.  AG-SPENDING (Washington) -- The House expects to finish work on a $62.7 billion agricultural spending bill for fiscal 1996, after disposing of controversial amendments today on whether to abolish a $110 million program that subsidizes food company advertising overseas.  Canada  INTERBREW-LABATT (Toronto) -- Interbrew SA moved a step closer to acquiring John Labatt Ltd. after the Canadian brewer's shareholders approved its C$4 billion (US$2.94 billion) takeover offer.  SPOTLIGHT-KMARTCANADA (Toronto) -- While Kmart struggles to keep its sizable share of the U.S. retail market, its Canadian unit is fighting to survive. Analysts believe Kmart may try to sell its Canadian unit to raise cash.  Columns &AMP; Commentary  RATES-OF-RETURN (New York) -- In the crowded market for corporate debt, sometimes it pays to be different. Just ask the Tennessee Valley Authority, which this week crafted a $500 million bond issue wiht such unique features that it attracted a flock of new investors on one of the businest days for corpate bond sales in months. In another sale, Walt Disney Co. sold bonds whose interest rate is tied to the box office returns of summer movies such as ``Die Hard 3'' and ``While You Were Sleeping.''  US-OUTLOOK (Washington) -- When is an employee not an employee? When he's an independent contractor. That distinction is becoming increasingly blurred as many companies move toward hiring more workers as contractors -- paid by the project, with no employer-supplied health or retirement benefits, and responsible for their own quarterly Social Security tax payments. The Internal Revenue Service, meanwhile, is having a hard time cracking down on employers who blur the distinction.  CONGRESS-WATCH (Washington) -- Some advice for American taxpayers on the so-called fiscal ``train wreck'' that's due to come when U.S. debt reaches its statutory limit: Take it with a grain of salt. Investors in the U.S. bond market, on the other hand, may want to take a couple of aspirin. They're the ones on the tracks.  WHITE-HOUSE-WATCH (Washington) -- The most serious foreign policy dilemma facing President Bill Clinton and U.S. business today isn't in the Balkans; it's in China. Tensions that have been growing for more than a year now have spilled over into corporate America, putting at risk billions of dollars in U.S.-China contracts. Times News Service  MEXICO REACHES TENTATIVE CONCLUSION ON CARDINAL'S MURDER  MEXICO CITY (NYT) _ After more than two years of investigation, the questioning of scores of witnesses and the pursuit of hundreds of leads, the Mexican authorities say they have reached a tentative conclusion about the murder of the Roman Catholic cardinal of Guadalajara.  Government officials say they still believe a group of drug traffickers shot the cardinal by mistake, confusing the tall, robed cleric with a short, rival trafficer. And most Mexicans, it appears, still don't believe them.  ``We do not agree with that hypothesis,'' the cleric's successor, Cardinal Juan Sandoval Iniguez(tilde over nn) said through a spokesman. ``We have never believed in the idea of a confusion, and we hope they will someday solve the case.''  COUPLE SUES ROMANIAN GOVERNMENT FOR CAUSING CHILD'S HIV INFECTION  IASI, Romania (NYT) _ Challenging a government with one of the worst records for AIDS prevention in Europe, a Romanian couple is suing the Romanian Health Ministry for causing the HIV infection of their daughter.  The case of Iasmina Calinciuc, a wide-eyed six year old girl with a loopy grin and a great fondness for TNT cartoons, has attracted wide attention and encouraged other parents to take on the Romanian Health Ministry for failing to eliminate what critcs describe as sloppy practices that cause the needless spread of AIDS.  Iasmina's case got to court for the simple reason that, unlike the thousands of Romania's orphans who were infected with the HIV virus during the communist dictatorship of Nicolae Ceausescu, she has parents willing to sue on her behalf.  BREATHING LIFE INTO A 1,000-YEAR-OLD SUPERMAN  BISHKEK, Kyrgyzstan (NYT) _ The 1,000-year-old legend of Manas has riveted this formerly Soviet, Central Asian nation where people say they have lost faith in just about everything else.  His birthday is being celebrated this week in a giant fair, including the construction of Manas Village, a government-sponsored ``authentic'' setting that, for some reason, employs pseudo-Roman architecture that might look at home in Disney World.  The legend of Manas has lived on here through an oral epic 20 times longer than the Iliad and Odyssey combined. The hero is a sort of divine superman, possessing the strength of Hercules and the avenging spirit of David. Using his omnipotent powers, Manas defeats foreign invaders and creates a ninth-century Kyrgyz superpower. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950830.0469 type=story > <HEADLINE> SPURRED BY TORONTO, MONTREAL LAYS ON A FEAST OF CINEMA </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> MONTREAL (BC-FILM-FESTIVALS-2TAKES-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199508.gz"

HEALTH-CUTS (Undated) _ Millions of working people and their employers will end up paying more for health care as a result of cuts that Congress is making in Medicare and Medicaid spending, economists and Washington business lobbyists said. The higher costs are a little discussed side effect of the legislation that would mainly be felt by the 130 million people covered by traditional fee-for-service health plans. Milt Freudenheim. f. 9 p.m.  3-column photo, of Yeltsin, with RUSSIA-YELTSIN; story is inside.  FOLD:  NONPROFIT-LOBBYING (Washington) _ The Istook Amendment, named for Rep. Ernest J. Istook Jr., a 45-year-old Mormon from Oklahoma, would restrict government lobbying by some non-profit groups that receive federal grants, legislating an end to what Istook calls welfare for lobbyists. Jerry Gray. w. 9 p.m.  YUGOSLAVIA-CROATIA (Sarajevo) _ Mostar, a 500-year-old city in a narrow, mountainous valley, was supposed to be the showcase of the Bosnian-Croat federation forged by Washington. It is the city where the West has worked hardest to reunify people who fought bitterly before a truce early in 1994. But today it remains as divided as Berlin once was. The Croats who govern the west side of Mostar refuse to allow the Muslims, who live in the east, where the Bosnian government has control, to cross the Neverta River. If anything, it appears, these are two nations moving apart, not together. They have different license plates, currency, and passports. Raymond Bonner. i. 9 p.m.  BOTTOM:  POWELL-POLITICS (Undated) _ There has been a veritable fusillade of telephone calls in the last couple of weeks from the general to friends, family members, governors, potential-fund-raisers, former cabinet members, and onetime colleagues at the Pentagon and the White House, seeking advice. Powell had a reputation as a hyper-cautious officer, stringing out decisions until the last possible moment, weighing every factor, and he is proving to be that kind of politician-in-the-making. (Skedded earlier as POWELL). R.W. Apple Jr. a. 8 p.m.  RUSSIA-BANKNOTES (Moscow) _ It is unlikely that most Americans will pay much attention when the U.S. Treasury introduces a redesigned $100 bill in January. The old bill will still be accepted everywhere, of course, and it's not exactly as if hundred-dollar notes are the coin of the realm. But things couldn't be more different here, because Russians are completely obsessed with the C-note. They covet, hoard, and trade them more than in any country other than America itself. Despite the fact that it is illegal to use dollars for retail trade, people who can scrape them together routinely use hundred-dollar bills to buy cars, trucks, or houses. Whenever they can get away with it, wage earners demand their salaries in hundreds, too. Michael Specter. i. 9 p.m.  MISS-POLITICS (Starkville, Miss.) _ If it is still possible for a progressive Democrat to win statewide office in the Deep South, the pollsters and consultants say, it is by forging a coalition between black voters and white women. That theorem will meet a significant test in next Tuesday's gubernatorial election in Mississippi. Kevin Sack. a. 9 p.m.  INSIDE STORIES PROMOTED ON PAGE 1:  ECON-REPORT (Washington) _ The nation's unemployment rate dipped to 5.5 percent in October, the lowest level in seven months, as moderate gains in construction and medical services helped to offset the effects of an aircraft manufacturing strike. (Skedded earlier as ECONOMY.) Robert D. Hershey Jr. f. 9 p.m.  KENTUCKY-POLITICS (Hopkinsville) _ Kentucky this year has a divided and somewhat disengaged electorate. Larry Forgy is challenging Paul Patton, the Democratic lieutenant-governor, to succeed Brereton C. Jones and end 24 consecutive years of Democrats in the Governor's Mansion. Until a recent change in state law, a Kentucky governor was barred from seeking re-election. While independent polls gave Patton comfortable leads early in the campaign, more recent surveys show that Forgy has pulled virtually even. Michael Janofsky. a. 9 p.m.  JAPAN-U.S. (Ginowan, Okinawa) _ To listen to Okinawans, the American military presence here is not a source of comfort or protection, but an annoyance and a threat. The result is a growing outcry to evict the American troops and American bases from Japan, or at least curb their presence. The uproar, backed by a recent protest of more than 50,000 demonstrators, is threatening to spoil the summit meeting this month between President Clinton and Prime Minister Tomiichi Murayama. Sheryl WuDunn. f. 8 p.m.  NY-UNWILLING-JUDGES (Undated) _ Christopher Renzulli is a 29-year-old lawyer who was sworn in as a member of the New York Bar in April. Under state law, he will not be eligible to run for judge until the year 2005. Yet Renzulli, a resident of Westchester County, will appear on Tuesday's ballot as a Conservative candidate for Bronx Supreme Court justice _ a job he did not seek and does not want. Renzulli is one of a handful of unwitting candidates who were nominated for judge without their consent or knowledge by underdog political parties bidding for some presence in this year's races. a. 9:30 p.m.  MIDWIVES (Hartford, Conn.) _ Connecticut health officials are trying a midwife, who has delivered 175 babies for practicing without a license. But she and her supporters say what is really under attack is a woman's control over her own pregnancy, and especially the option of having a home birth. a. 8 p.m. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19951103.0421 type=story > <HEADLINE> PHILIP MORRIS SHARES BREAK RECORD SET THREE YEARS AGO (UPDATE2) </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> New York, Nov. 3 (Bloomberg) (BC-PHILIP-MORRIS-REBOUND-UPDATE2-BLOOM) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199511.gz"

Times News Service for Thursday, Jan. 11:  JAPAN-REBUILD (Kobe, Japan) _ Last Jan. 17, the world looked on in horror at images of Kobe's devastating earthquake. Among the casualties was the Takatori Catholic Church, a plain wooden structure that was leveled by a fire that followed the quake, leaving the congregation of 600 Vietnamese immigrants, ethnic Koreans and poor Japanese with little hope of restoring their sanctuary.  By the fall, the congregation was able to celebrate Mass with a roof over its head, albeit one made of fabric. By Naomi Pollock.  VACANT-CHIC (Undated) _ What is the latest status symbol of the ultra rich? A spectacular residence you purchase for millions, reconfigure with the world's leading architects and interior designers for even more millions, then elect not to live in. By Christopher Mason.  TRIBECA (New York) _ Has the sun set on SoHo as a shopping destination for discriminating and artistic palates? Its streets are seeing an increasing numbers of businesses whose sister stores are not in Paris or Tokyo but in suburban shopping malls. Lower rents in Chelsea have already lured several well-regarded art galleries and high-end furniture and antique stores are looking elsewhere as well, from the meat-packing district to Little Italy. For many, the best frontier of all, it appears, is TriBeCa. By David Coleman.  HOME-WARRANTIES (Undated) _ Home warranties _ a kind of insurance or service contract that the seller, buyer or real estate agent buys from a warranty company _ are now being suggested by more and more real estate agents as a good way for sellers to attract buyers as a way for buyers to minimize the amount of money they spend after moving in. By Carole Burns.  RUBBLE-BUYER (Undated) _ Maine farmers start smiling when Lew French shows up on their back 40s. So do farmers in New Hampshires and the Berkshire Hills, because for a dozen years French, a 38-year-old stone mason, has paid them good money _ $10 a ton _ for the privilege of hauling away the plow-breaking, back-wrenching rocks their ancestors cursed. By John Budris.  BLACK-DECO (Undated) _ Most people know that Picasso and Modigliani collected African masks, which inspired their paintings in Europe during the 1930's. Yet few know much about the African-American influence on architecture and design during the Art Deco era. But it was there, in the United States and abroad. And the Miami Design Preservation League means to enlighten its audiences this weekend at a celebration of the contribution of African-Americans at the 19th Art Deco Weekend Festival in Miami Beach. By Jane Goldberg.  STANDARD FEATURES  HOME-IMPROVEMENT (Undated) _ Ridding the Roof of Ice Dams. By Edward R. Lipinski.  GARDENING-Q&AMP;A (Undated) _ Gardening questions. By Linda Yang. MONTINI-COLUMN (Undated) _ Outside the Southside Presbyterian Church in Tucson, near a large, hand-painted sign reading ``Este es el Santuario para los Oprimidos de Centro America'' _ ``This is a sanctuary for the oppressed of Central America'' _ young girls attached red, yellow, green and white paper flowers to a spindly wire fence. It was May 2, 1986, the day after eight of 11 defendants, including the church's pastor, Rev. John Fife, were found guilty in federal court of transporting illegal aliens, conspiracy and other charges. By E.J. MONTINI. Story will move in the ``A'' and ``K'' categories.  FOURPEAKSFIRE (Roosevelt, Ariz.) _ The thick pall of smoke over much of metropolitan Phoenix on Monday begins here, on the eastern slope of rugged Four Peaks, the logistical center of a fire fighting operation costing as much as $500,000 per day. Extending over 30,000 acres by Monday afternoon, the human-caused fire is the first wild fire of the dry season. By CHUCK HAWLEY. Story will move in the ``A'' category.  PROP187 (Phoenix) _ A proposed Arizona initiative to curb illegal immigration would make employers who knowingly hire illegal immigrants legally liable and would create a state ``verification center'' to determine whether job applicants have legal documents. The initiative, ``Save Our State-Az'' and patterned after California's Proposition 187, was filed Monday with the Arizona Secretary of State's Office. By MARK SHAFFER. Story will move in the ``A'' category.  BOMBECK (Phoenix) _ In Bermuda shorts and a straw hat, Joe Mudd of Phoenix, an avowed Erma Bombeck fan, watched Monday as pallbearers moved the humor columnist's coffin into St. Thomas the Apostle Catholic Church. ``I loved her for her goodness,'' he said. ``And I wouldn't have missed this for anything.'' Mudd was among 1,500 family members, friends and fans who came to say goodbye to the Ohio housewife-turned-humor columnist. By GAIL TABOR and LINDA HELSER. Story will move in the ``A'' category.  ARCHER-COLUMN (Undated) _ In the wake of Oklahoma City, President Clinton blamed ``promoters of paranoia'' for spreading ``hate'' and ``bitter words.'' Others were even less partisanly delicate, blaming those opposed to massive government regulation, high taxes and law enforcement excesses for inspiring the tragedy. For his spoils, Jarrett pocketed a whopping $564,035. ``To win here, it's a dream,'' said Jarrett, the only driver eligible to collect a $1 million bonus for winning three of the four Winston Million races (Daytona, Charlotte, Talladega and Darlington). ``To win Daytona, Charlotte and (Indy) in one year is just incredible. Reflecting back, and knowing where I came from, to get to this point is very gratifying. I don't know why God decided this is Dale Jarrett's year, but we may as well go ahead and win some more and take that million off their hands.'' ... After being injured in a terrible wall crash at Talladega, where he fractured his left collarbone and cracked his sternum, Dale Earnhardt gallantly rebounded to qualify 12th for the Brickyard 400, take the green flag and drive six laps before pitting on Lap 7 to relinquish his ride to Mike Skinner. ``I wasn't that nervous,'' said Skinner, whose 15th-place finish enabled Earnhardt to collect 123 points and remain in second place (2,731 points) in the Winston Cup race behind Terry Labonte (2,792). ``The nervousness that I had was for wrecking Dale's car.'' NEWS  YEAR-AULD-BOS (Undated) _ When Robert Burns raised a cup o' kindness to those of auld acquaintance, he suggested they not be forgot, for auld lang syne. Ella Fitzgerald died in 1996. She was the ``first lady of song,'' perhaps because people could listen to her all day long. Gene Kelly brought Irish sass and working-class brass to the art of dance. George Burns, who brought an impishly beguiling straight-man role to vaudeville, radio, television and movies, died at 100. By Martin F. Nolan. Moved 12-30.  BUSINESS  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-WINNERS-BLOOM (New York) _ In a year dominated by mega-mergers and flamboyant chief executives, IBM Chairman Louis Gerstner was the year's biggest winner by sticking to an old rule: Pay attention to your customers. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-1996-BLOOM (New York) _ The U.S. stock market's sixth straight annual gain led this year's rally in most equities worldwide, spurring companies to set records for mergers, acquisitions and initial public offerings. By Adam Levy. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-FINANCE-BLOOM (Undated) _ Soaring stocks, low interest rates and rising corporate profits made 1996 the biggest year for finance and mergers. Stock and bond sales rose to a record $419 billion, beating 1993's record of $388 billion. Mergers topped $1 trillion, shattering last year's $866 billion record. By Beth William. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-REVIEW-STOCKS-BLOOM (New York) _ The U.S. stock market's sixth straight annual gain led this year's rally in most equities worldwide, spurring companies to set records for mergers, acquisitions and initial public offerings. By Adam Levy. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-IPOS-BLOOM (New York) _ From Deutsche Telekom AG and Lucent Technologies Inc. to celebrity-backed Planet Hollywood International Inc. and chichi Saks Holdings Inc., 1996 was dominated by technology and retail initial public offerings. By Duncan Martell. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-MERGERS-BLOOM (Undated) _ Corporate mergers around the globe surged 25 percent to a record $1.04 trillion in 1996, propelled by record stock prices and low interest rates. By Katherine Burton. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-TRIVIA-BLOOM (Undated) _ How many trivia questions can you answer about the year in business and finance? Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-SALES-BLOOM (Undated) _ Soaring stocks, low interest rates and rising corporate profits made 1996 the biggest year for finance and mergers. By Beth William. Moved 12-24.  COMPUTER-COLUMN-$ADV29-HNS (Undated) _ The computer industry continued to race along in 1996, churning out at an even more feverish pace goodies for you and me to buy. Deciding which of these are worthy of the Computing column's annual Attaboy Awards is a tough job, but someone's gotta do it. By Dwight Silverman. Moved 12-26.  YEAR-ADS-MEDIA-NYT (Undated) _ A recap of the peaks and valleys of 1996 in the advertising world. A year of unprecedented turmoil on Madison Avenue produced many ads that seem fresher, sharper and better than the dreary and formulaic campaigns of the early '90s. But there were also scads of stinkers. By Stuart Elliott. Moved 12-26.  YEAR-CARS-NYT (Undated) _ Four automotive writers choose the cars they drove in 1996 that stood out, for good or ill, from the pack. The picks do not represent a list of the best or most significant vehicles of the year, though in many cases it would be hard to argue otherwise. They obviously reflect personal styles and preferences, but in each case the vehicles listed made an impression above and beyond many others. Moved 12-27 for release 12-29.  YEAR-BIZ-AWARDS-NYT (Undated) _ It was a marvelous year in the stock market, and voters showed they were no longer angry by re-electing almost every incumbent. That set off the best post-election rally since 1928. In such a year, there were many outstanding performances in the business world deserving of awards. By Floyd Norris. Moved 12-27 for release 12-29.  MOVIES  YEAR-FILM-NYT (Undated) _ The year in film: big events, little gems, lots of Shakespeare. By Linda Lee. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-MOVIES-BOS (Undated) _ Look at the most interesting films of 1996, and it's amazing to think that at one time most of the world defined film as Hollywood. Most of today's best movies come from places other than Hollywood. By Jay Carr. Moved 12-30.  TOPFILMS-96-KAN (Undated) _ This year's choices of films that opened in Kansas City in 1996 run the gamut from easygoing comedy to searing social drama to a radical reinterpretation of Shakespeare. They stand out because they don't rely on cliche and convention, because they dare to be different at a time when _ if box office figures are any indication _ audiences are interested only in the superficial, the bland and the easily assimilated. By Robert W. Butler. Moved 12-30.  FILMS-YEAR-SPI (Undated) _ The American movie business at year's end seemed a troubled industry, uncertain exactly who its audience is, assaulted by a gauntlet of unpleasant pressures, mystified as to why nine out of 10 films it released in 1996 seemed to go belly up on opening weekend. By William Arnold. Moved 12-26.  TOP-10-MOVIES-NYT (Undated) _ This year in film seemed more like two. The most memorable films of 1996 suggested parallel universes, one light and one fascinatingly dark. A mordant outlook, along with chilly detachment and often pitch-black humor, was the year's most pervasive force. By Janet Maslin. Moved 12-29.  TELEVISION  YEAR-TV (Undated) _ The year in TV: the large picture gets a little bit fuzzier. By Will Joyner. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-TV-BOS (Undated) _ A medium not known for its sincerity gave cause for more than the usual eye-rolling this year. By Frederic M. Biddle.Moved 12-30.  TV-WISHLIST-NYT (New York) _ To make the new year even happier, the most creative minds in television are about to show off approaching treats to the press in Hollywood. I am staying home, but I have my dreams. By Walter Goodman. Moved 12-30.  YEAR-TV-SPORTS-NYT (New York) _ Looking back on 1996 gives us a handle on what to expect in 1997. By Richard Sandomir. Moved 12-30.  ARTS  YEAR-CLASSICAL-MUSIC-NYT (Undated) _ The year in classical music: strikes, celebrations, a lot of Brahms. By James R. Oestreich. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-MUSIC-BOS (Undated) _ Like most years in this musical city, 1996 was a great one for music. The unbreakable three-way tie for the best concerto performance could arguably be matched in any of the other categories _ even opera, and this is supposed to be an opera-starved area! By Richard Dyer. Moved 12-30.  YEAR-THEATER-NYT (New York) _ The year in theater: new musicals, new stages quicken the pulse of Broadway. By Andrea Stevens. W/photos. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-STAGE-BOS (Undated) _ While all the hype this season was about the Wilbur and Shubert theaters' reopening with national road-company premieres of ``Master Class'' and ``Rent,'' I'll bet the most satisfied theatergoers were those coming out of the Colonial and Emerson Majestic after seeing Anna Deavere Smith and Spalding Gray. By Ed Siegel. Moved 12-30.  YEAR-IN-ART-NYT (New York) _ The year in art: praising famous men, and an empire or two. By Annette Grant. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-DANCE-NYT (Undated) _ The year in dance: burying the beloved, breaking fresh ground. By Will Joyner. W/photos. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-ART-BOS (Undated) _ The history of dance is ephemeral, which is why the opening of the Jacob's Pillow Archives at the famed Berkshire dance festival this summer is so important to the art form. By Christine Temin.Moved 12-30.  YEAR-ARCHITECTURE-NYT (New York) _ The year in architecture: renewal in New York, thinking big beyond. By Nora Kerr. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  ENTERTAINMENT  YEAR-ENTERTAINMENT-BOS (Undated) _ The pop game was a high-stakes sport in 1996, the year heroin became a series of celebrated ad campaigns and a hit movie, the year bullets took down rapper Tupac Shakur and made his phrase ``Thug Life'' oxymoronic, the year Michael Jackson chose nothing less than fatherhood as his next PR gambit. When we weren't watching analysis of the real-life ``O.J. Simpson Murders: The Sequel,'' we were charmed by world-threatening images from science-fiction phenomena like ``The X-Files'' and ``Independence Day,'' which rode into the all-time box-office Top 10 on footage of the White House collapsing. Rockers R.E.M. began a new national adventure in high finance by signing the fattest recording deal ever ($80 million for five albums), and with Jackie O.'s auction, America's idealized Camelot era was symbolically disassembled and scattered like so many ashes around the world. By Matthew Gilbert.Moved 12-30.  YEAR-POP-BOS (Undated) _ Alanis, Alanis, Alanis. Tell me, do you ever want to hear that name again? To say nothing of hearing her ``play'' the harmonica ...By Jim Sullivan. Moved 12-30.  YEAR-POP-JAZZ-MUSIC (Undated) _ The year in pop and jazz: as a dance caught fire, a cold killing. By Fletcher Roberts. Mvoed 12-24 for release 12-29.  POPYEAR-AZR (Undated) _ Rock turned a deaf year to having fun. By Salvatore Caputo. W/POPALBUMS-AZR. Moved 12-28 for release 12-29.  YEAR-POP-WOMAN-BOS (Undated) _ Time to call 1996 what it was: pop music's Year of the Woman. While testosterone-fueled bands had their ups and downs, women in rock 'n' roll, pop, country and folk often made the most dramatic impact in their fields. The super-gifted, super-charged Alanis Morissette became a household name. So did post-Streisand pop queen Celine Dion, whose ``Falling Into You'' album sold 5 million copies to become the year's top-selling release. By Steve Morse.Moved 12-30.  MUSIC-YEAR-BOS (Undated) _ Years come; years go; year-end wrap-ups will be with us always. Think of them as the Ebola virus of the media. Attempts to escape these encapsulations are futile. This, however, my 17th annual It's Only Rock 'n' Roll Awards, is less ``what did it all mean?'' than an attempt to honor the few, the proud, the rocking _ and pelt the charlatans with fruit and vegetables. As always, we operate under the motto we stole from a long-ago Streetwalkers album title: ``Vicious, but fair.'' By Jim Sullivan. Moved 12-27.  SPORTS  YEAR-ANDERSON-COLUMN-NYT (Undated) _ After the revelry, reality returned. Not long after a celebration, a comedown usually occurred. And even with the glory, gloom often hovered. In sports in 1996, many victories were never enjoyed for long and the losses lingered. By Dave Anderson. Moved 12-28.  YEAR-LIPSYTE-COLUMN-NYT (Undated) _ I AM TIGER WOODS. If that's going to be the sports/advertising slogan of 1997, it's going to be my personal mantra, too. By Robert Lipsyte.Moved 12-28.  YEAR-VECSEY-COLUMN-NYT (New York) _ For a roaming columnist who lives for exciting and exotic datelines, 1996 was never going to be a great year, once the Olympic delegates handed the Summer Games over to Atlanta. Then the Yankees _ bless their transient little hearts _ made sure I would be spending much of September and October on the Cross Bronx Expressway. However, sticking close to home has its advantages. By George Vecsey. Moved 12-28.  FASHION  96FASHION-WHITE-KAN (Undated) _ Kick up your chunky boot heels. Hike your skinny pants (if they aren't too tight). Tuck in your shiny shirt. Flick the lint from your brown velvet. And, cheerfully, folks, just let go of 1996. By Jackie White. Moved 12-30.  COMMENTARY  YEARS-INS-AND-OUTS-BOS (Undated) _ Ah, but who or what was truly above suspicion? No one, that's who. Not in a year when going negative was In and going public was Out. When same-sex marriages were In and schoolyard smooching was Out. When trainspotting was In and ump-spitting was Out. When lawyering up was In and dumbing down was Out. When tickling Elmo was In and toe sucking was... ``Didn't do it,'' said Texaco executives. ``Not us,'' said the Clinton administration. ``Wrong shoes,'' said O.J. ``Check out John Huang.'' By Joseph P. Kahn. Moved 12-31. By Adam Levy. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-FINANCE-BLOOM (Undated) _ Soaring stocks, low interest rates and rising corporate profits made 1996 the biggest year for finance and mergers. Stock and bond sales rose to a record $419 billion, beating 1993's record of $388 billion. Mergers topped $1 trillion, shattering last year's $866 billion record. By Beth William. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-REVIEW-STOCKS-BLOOM (New York) _ The U.S. stock market's sixth straight annual gain led this year's rally in most equities worldwide, spurring companies to set records for mergers, acquisitions and initial public offerings. By Adam Levy. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-IPOS-BLOOM (New York) _ From Deutsche Telekom AG and Lucent Technologies Inc. to celebrity-backed Planet Hollywood International Inc. and chichi Saks Holdings Inc., 1996 was dominated by technology and retail initial public offerings. By Duncan Martell. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-MERGERS-BLOOM (Undated) _ Corporate mergers around the globe surged 25 percent to a record $1.04 trillion in 1996, propelled by record stock prices and low interest rates. By Katherine Burton. Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-TRIVIA-BLOOM (Undated) _ How many trivia questions can you answer about the year in business and finance? Moved 12-24.  YEAR-IN-REVIEW-SALES-BLOOM (Undated) _ Soaring stocks, low interest rates and rising corporate profits made 1996 the biggest year for finance and mergers. By Beth William. Moved 12-24.  COMPUTER-COLUMN-$ADV29-HNS (Undated) _ The computer industry continued to race along in 1996, churning out at an even more feverish pace goodies for you and me to buy. Deciding which of these are worthy of the Computing column's annual Attaboy Awards is a tough job, but someone's gotta do it. By Dwight Silverman. Moved 12-26.  YEAR-ADS-MEDIA-NYT (Undated) _ A recap of the peaks and valleys of 1996 in the advertising world. A year of unprecedented turmoil on Madison Avenue produced many ads that seem fresher, sharper and better than the dreary and formulaic campaigns of the early '90s. But there were also scads of stinkers. By Stuart Elliott. Moved 12-26.  YEAR-CARS-NYT (Undated) _ Four automotive writers choose the cars they drove in 1996 that stood out, for good or ill, from the pack. The picks do not represent a list of the best or most significant vehicles of the year, though in many cases it would be hard to argue otherwise. They obviously reflect personal styles and preferences, but in each case the vehicles listed made an impression above and beyond many others. Moved 12-27 for release 12-29.  YEAR-BIZ-AWARDS-NYT (Undated) _ It was a marvelous year in the stock market, and voters showed they were no longer angry by re-electing almost every incumbent. That set off the best post-election rally since 1928. In such a year, there were many outstanding performances in the business world deserving of awards. By Floyd Norris. Moved 12-27 for release 12-29.  MOVIES  YEAR-FILM-NYT (Undated) _ The year in film: big events, little gems, lots of Shakespeare. By Linda Lee. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-MOVIES-BOS (Undated) _ Look at the most interesting films of 1996, and it's amazing to think that at one time most of the world defined film as Hollywood. Most of today's best movies come from places other than Hollywood. By Jay Carr. Moved 12-30.  TOPFILMS-96-KAN (Undated) _ This year's choices of films that opened in Kansas City in 1996 run the gamut from easygoing comedy to searing social drama to a radical reinterpretation of Shakespeare. They stand out because they don't rely on cliche and convention, because they dare to be different at a time when _ if box office figures are any indication _ audiences are interested only in the superficial, the bland and the easily assimilated. By Robert W. Butler. Moved 12-30.  FILMS-YEAR-SPI (Undated) _ The American movie business at year's end seemed a troubled industry, uncertain exactly who its audience is, assaulted by a gauntlet of unpleasant pressures, mystified as to why nine out of 10 films it released in 1996 seemed to go belly up on opening weekend. By William Arnold. Moved 12-26.  TOP-10-MOVIES-NYT (Undated) _ This year in film seemed more like two. The most memorable films of 1996 suggested parallel universes, one light and one fascinatingly dark. A mordant outlook, along with chilly detachment and often pitch-black humor, was the year's most pervasive force. By Janet Maslin. Moved 12-29.  TELEVISION  YEAR-TV (Undated) _ The year in TV: the large picture gets a little bit fuzzier. By Will Joyner. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-TV-BOS (Undated) _ A medium not known for its sincerity gave cause for more than the usual eye-rolling this year. By Frederic M. Biddle.Moved 12-30.  TV-WISHLIST-NYT (New York) _ To make the new year even happier, the most creative minds in television are about to show off approaching treats to the press in Hollywood. I am staying home, but I have my dreams. By Walter Goodman. Moved 12-30.  YEAR-TV-SPORTS-NYT (New York) _ Looking back on 1996 gives us a handle on what to expect in 1997. By Richard Sandomir. Moved 12-30.  ARTS  YEAR-CLASSICAL-MUSIC-NYT (Undated) _ The year in classical music: strikes, celebrations, a lot of Brahms. By James R. Oestreich. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-MUSIC-BOS (Undated) _ Like most years in this musical city, 1996 was a great one for music. The unbreakable three-way tie for the best concerto performance could arguably be matched in any of the other categories _ even opera, and this is supposed to be an opera-starved area! By Richard Dyer. Moved 12-30.  YEAR-THEATER-NYT (New York) _ The year in theater: new musicals, new stages quicken the pulse of Broadway. By Andrea Stevens. W/photos. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-STAGE-BOS (Undated) _ While all the hype this season was about the Wilbur and Shubert theaters' reopening with national road-company premieres of ``Master Class'' and ``Rent,'' I'll bet the most satisfied theatergoers were those coming out of the Colonial and Emerson Majestic after seeing Anna Deavere Smith and Spalding Gray. By Ed Siegel. Moved 12-30.  YEAR-IN-ART-NYT (New York) _ The year in art: praising famous men, and an empire or two. By Annette Grant. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-DANCE-NYT (Undated) _ The year in dance: burying the beloved, breaking fresh ground. By Will Joyner. W/photos. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  YEAR-ART-BOS (Undated) _ The history of dance is ephemeral, which is why the opening of the Jacob's Pillow Archives at the famed Berkshire dance festival this summer is so important to the art form. By Christine Temin.Moved 12-30.  YEAR-ARCHITECTURE-NYT (New York) _ The year in architecture: renewal in New York, thinking big beyond. By Nora Kerr. Moved 12-24 for release 12-29.  ENTERTAINMENT  YEAR-ENTERTAINMENT-BOS (Undated) _ The pop game was a high-stakes sport in 1996, the year heroin became a series of celebrated ad campaigns and a hit movie, the year bullets took down rapper Tupac Shakur and made his phrase ``Thug Life'' oxymoronic, the year Michael Jackson chose nothing less than fatherhood as his next PR gambit. When we weren't watching analysis of the real-life ``O.J. Simpson Murders: The Sequel,'' we were charmed by world-threatening images from science-fiction phenomena like ``The X-Files'' and ``Independence Day,'' which rode into the all-time box-office Top 10 on footage of the White House collapsing. Rockers R.E.M. began a new national adventure in high finance by signing the fattest recording deal ever ($80 million for five albums), and with Jackie O.'s auction, America's idealized Camelot era was symbolically disassembled and scattered like so many ashes around the world. By Matthew Gilbert.Moved 12-30.  YEAR-POP-BOS (Undated) _ Alanis, Alanis, Alanis. Tell me, do you ever want to hear that name again? To say nothing of hearing her ``play'' the harmonica ...By Jim Sullivan. Moved 12-30.  YEAR-POP-JAZZ-MUSIC (Undated) _ The year in pop and jazz: as a dance caught fire, a cold killing. By Fletcher Roberts. Mvoed 12-24 for release 12-29.  POPYEAR-AZR (Undated) _ Rock turned a deaf year to having fun. By Salvatore Caputo. W/POPALBUMS-AZR. Moved 12-28 for release 12-29.  YEAR-POP-WOMAN-BOS (Undated) _ Time to call 1996 what it was: pop music's Year of the Woman. While testosterone-fueled bands had their ups and downs, women in rock 'n' roll, pop, country and folk often made the most dramatic impact in their fields. The super-gifted, super-charged Alanis Morissette became a household name. So did post-Streisand pop queen Celine Dion, whose ``Falling Into You'' album sold 5 million copies to become the year's top-selling release. By Steve Morse.Moved 12-30.  MUSIC-YEAR-BOS (Undated) _ Years come; years go; year-end wrap-ups will be with us always. Think of them as the Ebola virus of the media. Attempts to escape these encapsulations are futile. This, however, my 17th annual It's Only Rock 'n' Roll Awards, is less ``what did it all mean?'' than an attempt to honor the few, the proud, the rocking _ and pelt the charlatans with fruit and vegetables. As always, we operate under the motto we stole from a long-ago Streetwalkers album title: ``Vicious, but fair.'' By Jim Sullivan. Moved 12-27.  SPORTS  YEAR-ANDERSON-COLUMN-NYT (Undated) _ After the revelry, reality returned. Not long after a celebration, a comedown usually occurred. And even with the glory, gloom often hovered. In sports in 1996, many victories were never enjoyed for long and the losses lingered. By Dave Anderson. Moved 12-28.  YEAR-LIPSYTE-COLUMN-NYT (Undated) _ I AM TIGER WOODS. If that's going to be the sports/advertising slogan of 1997, it's going to be my personal mantra, too. By Robert Lipsyte.Moved 12-28.  YEAR-VECSEY-COLUMN-NYT (New York) _ For a roaming columnist who lives for exciting and exotic datelines, 1996 was never going to be a great year, once the Olympic delegates handed the Summer Games over to Atlanta. Then the Yankees _ bless their transient little hearts _ made sure I would be spending much of September and October on the Cross Bronx Expressway. However, sticking close to home has its advantages. By George Vecsey. Moved 12-28.  FASHION  96FASHION-WHITE-KAN (Undated) _ Kick up your chunky boot heels. Hike your skinny pants (if they aren't too tight). Tuck in your shiny shirt. Flick the lint from your brown velvet. And, cheerfully, folks, just let go of 1996. By Jackie White. Moved 12-30.  COMMENTARY  YEARS-INS-AND-OUTS-BOS (Undated) _ Ah, but who or what was truly above suspicion? No one, that's who. Not in a year when going negative was In and going public was Out. When same-sex marriages were In and schoolyard smooching was Out. When trainspotting was In and ump-spitting was Out. When lawyering up was In and dumbing down was Out. When tickling Elmo was In and toe sucking was... ``Didn't do it,'' said Texaco executives. ``Not us,'' said the Clinton administration. ``Wrong shoes,'' said O.J. ``Check out John Huang.'' By Joseph P. Kahn. Moved 12-31. They stand out because they don't rely on cliche and convention, because they dare to be different at a time when _ if box office figures are any indication _ audiences are interested only in the superficial, the bland and the easily assimilated. By Robert W. Butler. Moved 12-30. ENTERTAINMENT  YEAR-ENTERTAINMENT-BOS (Undated) _ The pop game was a high-stakes sport in 1996, the year heroin became a series of celebrated ad campaigns and a hit movie, the year bullets took down rapper Tupac Shakur and made his phrase ``Thug Life'' oxymoronic, the year Michael Jackson chose nothing less than fatherhood as his next PR gambit. When we weren't watching analysis of the real-life ``O.J. Simpson Murders: The Sequel,'' we were charmed by world-threatening images from science-fiction phenomena like ``The X-Files'' and ``Independence Day,'' which rode into the all-time box-office Top 10 on footage of the White House collapsing. Rockers R.E.M. began a new national adventure in high finance by signing the fattest recording deal ever ($80 million for five albums), and with Jackie O.'s auction, America's idealized Camelot era was symbolically disassembled and scattered like so many ashes around the world. Following is a tentative sked:  KOREA-DOGS (Seoul) _ This is one of a number of places in Asia where man can cuddle up by a fire with his best friend, and in hungrier days, eat him or his kin. In South Korea, the term ``dog-lover'' is as ambiguous a phrase as ``dog food.'' As South Korea tries to gain a more important spot on the world stage, it is sometimes caught between old tastes and a yearning for respect from foreign countries. Many Koreans argue that it is no one's business but their own if they happen to eat dog, but there is still a broad current of embarrassment about the practice because people realize that Westerners may regard such dining habits as uncouth or morally offensive. A Foreign Journal by Sheryl WuDunn.  WASHINGTON  CONGRESS-GIFTS (Washington) _ A year after federal lawmakers imposed on themselves one of the most restrictive codes of conduct ever and on the eve of the start of the first full Congress to live under those regulations, they are beginning to feel the full bite of the rules. By Karen DeWitt.  NATIONAL GENERAL (``a'' code)  SEE-ROCK-CITY (Lookout Mountain, Ga.) _ They were the Sirens of the South, beckoning to generations of vacationers driving to Florida. Around the bend on U.S. Highway 41 out of Michigan, over the crest of a hill on Kentucky state highway 91, all of a sudden there would be a barn, splashed with giant white block letters: ``See Rock City.'' It was a masterful marketing campaign that grew up with the automobile, some 900 barns in 19 states. But times changed, and, ``The barns, they're all fading now,'' said Clark Byers, 81, who spent more than 30 years painting the signs.  NORTHWEST-STORM (Seattle) _ The Pacific Northwest finished off its holiday week with yet another powerful storm that brought drenching rain and high wind to the coast, mudslides in Seattle and flooding south of the city. On the southern Oregon coast, gusts of up to 100 mph whipped beach towns, toppling utility poles and trees and knocking out power to hundreds of people. And in tiny Guerneville, Calif., the Russian River is expected to reach a record crest of 49 feet, 17 feet above flood level. By Carey Goldberg.  DETROIT-KIDNAP (Detroit) _ Debra Iverson had everything: a notable career as an opthalmalogist, wealth and two beautiful children. Then one day, on her way to an upscale shopping mall, she disappeared. For a time her husband was a suspect, but now, it appears, she fell victim to a modern nightmare: a random kidnapping and murder by two young strangers.  ACCUSED-NAZI (Kansas City, Kan.) _ Michael Kolnhofer, was thought to be a quiet widower who made a pleasant neighbor. CITY STREETS  DEAR DIARY:  A couple we know briefed their 3-year-old granddaughter on the Nativity before traveling from New Jersey to see the Christmas pageant at Radio City Music Hall.  Our seats were well upstairs. So it was that amid the silence of the manger scene the youngster rose, waved her arms and shouted: ``Baby Jesus! It's me, Cameo. I'm up here!''  _ STANLEY H. KREITMAN  Another chapter from the annals of the New York City Subway Journal. At the 66th Street station on the IRT line, that mysterious ``sick passenger'' held up the morning rush hour by at least 20 minutes. The riders were getting restless, especially one extremely vexed fellow who was yelling at the token clerk. Just when the police arrived, the man shouted: ``I gotta get somewhere! This is called `rapid transit,' officers. Rapid transit!''  ``And this,'' the officer replied sternly, ``is called `calm down, nobody cares!' ''  A brief pause, then the man said, ``My therapist says it's good to vent.'' _ RITA D. POLIDORI  Dear Diary:  This past Christmas I resolved not to make anyone a sweater. Sure, I have closets full of yarn, springy spruce-green worsted, candy-cane red chenille, wispy white angora. I can whip up a winter woodland scene on a pullover. Or a cabled cardigan trimmed with gold metallic thread. Well, maybe, if I had started earlier. In June, say.  Why do we pretend to be pioneers around the holidays? Braiding rag rugs in a ragless age of paper towels? Ruining manicures by stringing popcorn and cranberry garlands like Laura Ingalls Wilder? Piecing together quilts even if we have to buy our scrap materials at the mall? While our grandmothers welcomed store-bought decorations as a sign of prosperity, we insist on making Christmas creations in front of the gas-powered fireplace.  Maybe modern women want to get in touch with the spirit of their ancestors as a comforting antidote to the stresses of the two-career family. The holidays provide a socially acceptable opportunity to ``stay home and bake cookies.'' It may be an expression of a softer, feminine side that is buried under publishing deadlines. Whatever the reason, I loved my craftless Christmas.  _ ANN TRACY  The time: Christmas week.  Place: The North American Mammals wing of the American Museum of Natural History.  Dramatis personae: 7-year-old boy talking to his 3-year-old sister, overheard by Mariana Nacht.  ``Did you know that a full-size moose is longer than two Volvos?''  Dear Diary:  I left New York reluctantly when I retired some years ago. Now I return two or three times a year for what I call my ``New York fix.'' A couple of weeks ago, on a beautiful day, I was walking up Fifth Avenue, gaping at Lord &AMP; Taylor. I was sublimely happy _ until I tripped and fell flat on my face.  As I lay sprawled on the sidewalk a kind voice said, ``Are you all right?''  ``Yes,'' I replied.  ``Why the hell don't you look where you're going?'' came the swift reply.  I love New York.  _ GEORGE DOOLITTLE  DAVID-LETTERMAN ``LATE SHOW'' TOP TEN  ``President Clinton Pick-Up Lines''  10. ``I bet you could use some hillbilly lovin'.''  9. ``Ever done it in a jacuzzi full of gravy?''  8. ``How would you like to become a minor footnote to history?''  7. ``I want you so bad, it's worth the inevitable multi-million dollar lawsuit.''  6. ``For a Secretary of State, you've got a great ass.''  5. ``Is your husband really fulfilling your needs, Mrs. Yeltsin?''  4. ``My wife's going to jail. Wanna party?''  3. ``Do you take Visa?''  2. ``I just ratified a bill -- how would you like to gratify a bill?''  1. ``Let's McDo it!''  HEADLINE HEADACHES  You might think of them as the missed notes in the symphony of American journalism.  Then again, you might not.  But you don't need to be able to tell the difference between a constipated goose and an out-of-tune oboe to detect some serious discordance in the following headlines:  Lung Cancer In Women Mushrooms _ This is tragic news, certainly, and we can only hope that medical science will someday find a cure. But I think the more important question here is whether the women mushrooms' condition makes them unsafe to eat.  Stolen Painting Found By Tree _ Everybody knows that trees provide shade and lumber, but this is the first time I've heard of one tracking down a stolen painting. Who knows? If they had had a competent, unbiased eucalyptus on the Los Angeles police force, O.J. Simpson might be behind bars.  Prostitutes Appeal To Pope _ What the heck. The guy's old. He's been shot. Let him have a little fun.  Man Struck By Lightning Faces Battery Charges _ This seems like a rather odd way to charge batteries, but if it works, why not? Personally, if I had been struck by lightning, I don't think my first thought would be, Hey! Let's go charge up some Diehards! Clearly, this guy has way too much energy.  Chef Throws His Heart Into Helping The Needy _ This is a rather disgusting example of a man who is much too nice for his own good. It's bad enough finding a hair in your soup, let alone someone's heart.  Bank Drive-In Window Blocked By Board _ So what's the big deal? Pick up the damn board and move it out of the way! Sheesh. People can be so helpless.  Lansing Residents Can Drop Off Trees _ Well, yes, I suppose they can, but why would they want to? Things must be pretty slow in Lansing if people are getting their kicks by dropping off trees. It sounds like a dumb idea to me. For one thing, you might hurt yourself. And the other thing is the tree might arrest you.  Prosecutor Releases Probe Into Undersheriff _ Ouch! I'm not quite sure what an undersheriff is, but I definitely don't want the job if that's the way they're treated.  Old School Pillars Are Replaced By Alumn _ Perhaps this is what happens to alumns who don't send the old school enough money, I don't know. Either way, it doesn't sound like a very satisfactory way to hold up a roof. Your arms and back would get really tired, but probably not as much as if you were a pillar of the community.  Arson Suspect Is Held In Massachusetts Fire _ This seems like a rather extreme form of interrogation. Then again, they used to burn witches in Salem, so maybe this is just the way things are done in Massachusetts.  Typhoon Rips Through Cemetery; Hundreds Dead _ Well, what do you expect to find in a cemetery? People dancing the Macarena? How about giving us some details about the typhoon?  Astronaut Takes Blame For Gas In Spacecraft _ That's what the astronaut gets for eating that pastrami-and-sauerkraut sandwich before takeoff. I mean, you can't exactly open a window up there, you know.  Kids Make Nutritious Snacks _ Well, so do grasshoppers and earthworms, but that doesn't mean we should eat them! Jeez, talk about your irresponsible journalism.  Include Your Children When Baking Cookies _ OK, that's it. I'm canceling my subscription.  _ From David Grimes, columnist for the Sarasota (Fla.) Herald-Tribune.)  BILL'S WORLD  by Bill Tammeus, The Kansas City Star  Super Bowl tip: Bet on a team with a nickname that begins Pa-something, as long as it's not the Panthers.  One of the official participants in presidential inauguration-related events will be Elmo from Sesame Street. Makes you wonder when someone will market a Tickle Me Paula Jones doll.  In the military's recent rehearsal of the inauguration, Mr. Clinton was played by a Marine sergeant. Obviously not type cast.  The Colorado Avalanche, winners of the 1996 Stanley Cup, visited the White House the other day. Team members must all be Democrats. None of them checked the president into a wall.  Huge amounts of snow fell in New York state last weekend. Wait until you see what Washington gets with the upcoming inaugural and State of the Union speeches.  A new survey shows 38 percent of college freshmen spend at least an hour a week doing volunteer work. Which may be more than the amount of sleep some of them get.  A new study shows smokers age faster than nonsmokers. All that nagging must take a toll.  A British newspaper says Buckingham Palace is planning a public relations campaign to convince folks Prince Charles will make a good king. And, it appears, a brief one, too.  Michael Jackson says he wants to make Scotland his home. Bet that's an idea some residents there want kilt.  Chinese scientists report a breakthrough in breeding better varieties of rice. Will it be marketed as Uncle Ben Xiaoping's?  GOOD DAY, WIRE WANDERERS!!!!!  Offer not available in any store! (Or any other news service!) NYT News Service  INTERNATIONAL _ (Moved in ``i'' category)  COLOMBIA-EXODUS (Bogota, Colombia) _ Frustrated with four years of recession and terrified by the crime and kidnappings that flourish in a grinding civil conflict, many of Colombia's most promising young professionals and business people have reluctantly abandoned this country in the hope of finding success and stability. Many have wound up in the United States, which the Census Bureau estimates has a Colombian-American community of 435,000 people. By Juan Forero. 1.400 words.  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  CHINA-U.S. (Beijing) _ Although China remains a one-party dictatorship, public opinion is increasingly a factor that must be taken into account by leaders who, in the absence of a charismatic figure like Mao or Deng Xiaoping, must also resolve internal disputes before resolving an issue as sensitive as the spy-plane standoff. By Elisabeth Rosenthal.  NATIONAL _ GENERAL (Moved in ``a'' category)  TEENS-RUSSIAN-ROULETTE (Indiana, Pa.) _ Court stories: The tale of two boys, a gun and the legal repercussions. By William Glaberson. 1,900 words.  OBIT-STARGELL (Undated) _ Obituary of Willie Stargell, former Pittsburgh Pirates slugger. By Richard Goldstein.  INDIGENT-DEFENSE-III (New York) _ For indigent defendants who believe that justice was not done in New York City's trial courts, appeals courts offer a measure of hope. The likelihood of success depends, at least in part, on the skill and effort of the lawyer appointed to handle the appeal. But just as there are problems with lawyers who defend the poor at trial, there are problems with the lawyers appointed to file appeals. Some work for nonprofit organizations and are paid salaries, but many work on their own. By Jane Fritsch and David Rohde. (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.) 2,200 words. BUSINESS DAY _ (Moved in ``f'' category)  BUSH-SUPPLY-SIDE (Washington) _ After a decade of relative anonymity, the people who call themselves supply-side economists now have a seat at the table of power. But their arguments do not compose the core of the Bush administration's rationale for a tax cut, as they did in the Reagan administration. Still, while none of President Bush's top advisers are considered true supply-siders, their basic philosophy has a good deal in common with the school of thought that was famously encapsulated by the Laffer curve. By David Leonhardt.  NIKE-AD-COLUMN (New York) _ A popular television commercial for basketball shoes is being turned into a spot that resembles a music video in a step that is likely to further blur the already wavy line between sponsored and nonsponsored content. By Stuart Elliott.  INDONESIA-ECON (Jakarta) _ When a team from the International Monetary Fund arrives here on Wednesday, it may feel a sense of deja vu. Indonesia's currency is tumbling, the economy is weakening and political instability is roiling the country. Much has certainly changed since the turbulent days of the Asian financial crisis. But analysts here say Indonesia stands at yet another perilous crossroads. By Mark Landler.  SAINSBURY-MIDEAST (London) _ The British supermarket group J. Sainsbury, whose stores in Egypt have been the target of a boycott of U.S. and Israeli products and attacks fueled by rumors that the chain was owned by a Jewish backer of Israel, said it will withdraw from Egypt at a cost of as much as $180 million to focus on its home market, Britain, and the United States. SCIENCE TIMES (Moved in ``a'' category)  SCI-DARK-ENERGY (Baltimore) _ Evidence is presented at an astronomical conference that many consider incontrovertible, based on observations of star that exploded 11 billion years ago: that the universe is being blown apart under the influence of a mysterious antigravitational force known only as ``dark energy,'' and that stars and galaxies are but mere specks of froth on a stormy sea of dark matter that outweighs the stuff of stars and people almost 10 to 1. By Dennis Overbye. (2800 words)  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  SCI-EXTREME-GALAXIES (Baltimore) _ Astronomers at the Fred Lawrence Whipple Observatory in Arizona announced at a conference here this month that they had apparently doubled, to four, the number of known ``extreme'' galaxies, which are oriented in the rarest and most revealing angle for earthly observers. Astronomers theorize that these galaxies are special because of powerful jets of particles, possibly accelerated by an unseen, massive black hole at the center, pointed directly at Earth from millions of light-years away. By James Glanz. (860 words)  (ART ADV: Photo NYT14 is being sent to NYT photo clients.)  SCI-SUNSPOTS (Undated) _ A tempestuous sun at the peak of its 11-year cycle of storms is lighting up dark skies around the globe with auroras that dance in shimmering waves of color reaching as far south as Mexico. The action picked up on March 29, when a dark, agitated region of the sun developed into the biggest sunspot of the current solar cycle. The spot was 14 times as large as than the Earth, and its seething tumult and associated flares soon had a number of terrestrial effects. By William J. Broad. (530 words)  (ART ADV: Photo NYT10 is being sent to NYT photo clients.)  SCI-YELLOWSTONE-FIRES (Yellowstone National Park, Wyo.) _ Yellowstone has proved to be a laboratory on forest fires. As a park scientist explains: ``Mother Nature does not destroy herself. It's that simple. Instead it is a giant reapportionment of species.'' Wildfires, in other words, cause shifts in habitat along a spectrum, from major re-structurings to subtle ones depending on factors that include the intensity of the burn and the condition of the soil. The changes favor some species and increase their distribution and abundance, while hampering others, sometimes for the short term, and sometimes for much longer periods. The park has detailed these effects in a new 118-page book called ``Yellowstone in the Afterglow: Lessons from the Fire.'' By Jim Robbins. (1950 words)  (ART ADV: Photo NYT9 and a graphic are being sent to NYT photo and graphic clients.)  SCI-VOGT-VIRUS (Undated) _ A Scientist at Work profile: Dr. Marguerite Vogt. Over the decades, Vogt has worked with Dr. Renato Dulbecco of the Salk Institute on groundbreaking studies of how the polio virus forms distinctive plaques in tissue culture, work that proved essential to the development of a polio vaccine. Later, the two researchers explored how a type of mammalian tumor virus called the polyoma virus transforms ordinary cells into cancer cells, research that lifted the study of cancer biology from a mere cataloging of the gross anatomy of a tumor cell to an exploration of the genetic mutations that underlie the disease. But while Dulbecco eventually won a Nobel Prize for his research, Vogt has yet to be awarded a single significant prize. By Natalie Angier. (2000 words)  SCI-WATCH (Undated) _ News and notes on science: Items on clues of salamander evolution from China, amphibian extinctions and their relationship to global warming, et alia. By Henry Fountain.  SCI-HAT-PROBLEM (Undated) _ It's no ordinary puzzle that's spreading through networks of mathematicians like a juicy bit of gossip. ``The hat problem'' is consuming brain cycles at universities and research labs across the country and has become a vibrant topic of discussion on the Internet. The reason this problem is so captivating, mathematicians say, is that it is not just a recreational puzzle to be solved and put away. Rather, it has deep and unexpected connections to coding theory, an active area of mathematical research with broad applications in telecommunications and computer science. By Sara Robinson. (1500 words)  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  SCI-SYNESTHESIA (Undated) _ People who claim to taste shapes, feel sounds and see numbers or letters in color are often dismissed as having particularly vivid imaginations. In two newly published reports, scientists demonstrated that the phenomenon, known as synesthesia, was genuine. On a variety of perceptual tests in which colors were mixed with numbers and letters, synesthetes performed more accurately and faster than people who did not experience sensory mixing. By Sandra Blakeslee. (750 words)  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  SCI-CANCER-THERAPY (Undated) _ A study involving arsenic as a treatment for leukemia is one of the recent successes of developmental chemotherapy, the field of cancer research dealing with human testing of unproven drugs. Because the National Institutes of Health randomly screens tens of thousands of compounds annually, the drugs tested in early human trials often have the flavor of the accidental or even bizarre. Paclitaxel, for example, one of the most successful new drugs in recent years, now widely used to treat breast cancer, was originally isolated from the bark of the Pacific yew tree. Besides arsenic, other heavy metals are being studied for anti-cancer activity, including antimony and mercury. In New York, trials are under way looking at high-dose Tylenol and green tea. By Sandeep Jauhar. (1885 words)  (ART ADV: Photos NYT 11,12,13 are being sent to NYt photo clients.)  SCI-SELF-IMAGE (Undated) _ ``I'm a better person than I used to be'' may seem the exclusive mantra of former presidents, chastened lovers and veterans of the analytic couch. But if a series of studies by two social psychologists offers any indication, the conviction that today's self is a new and improved version of yesterday's is, in fact, ubiquitous. Asked to evaluate themselves in the past and in the present, the researchers found, the subjects in their studies consistently reported that they were now more competent, more socially skilled, more tolerant, more polite, more mature and less boring than they had been in the past. Moreover, their own degree of improvement over time, the subjects revealed, was substantially greater than that of their friends and relatives. By Erica Goode. (975 words)  SCI-DYSLEXIA-LANGUAGE (Undated) _ Dyslexia _ or at least the seriousness of any given case _ may also be an accident not just of neurology but of geography, a new study suggests. Simply put: where a dyslexic is raised, and the language that is spoken there, may play a role in the severity of his disability. And English, it appears, owing to its notoriously fickle spelling, may be the most daunting. The findings were reported in a recent issue of the journal Science, and were based on work done by researchers in three countries: England, France and Italy. By Eric Nagourney. (800 words)  SCI-INFANT-DISEASE (Undated) _ A doctor encounters his first case of Cockayne's syndrome in a tiny infant. The milder form of this syndrome (Type I) is rare and horrific and those affected usually die in their teens or 20s. This child had Type II Cockayne's, the rarer (about 30 reported cases) and more severe form (death usually occurs within the first few years of life). Cockayne's syndrome occurs because of a defect in the process of repairing RNA. By Michael Rosenbaum, M.D. (900 words)  SCI-BRODY-HEALTH (Undated) _ The Personal Health column: Thousands of unwanted pregnancies and subsequent abortions could be prevented with what experts describe as one of medicine's best-kept secrets: the morning after pill. By Jane E. Brody. (1280 words)  SCI-VITAL-SIGNS (Undated) _ News and notes on health and fitness:  _ Doctors often prescribe antibiotics for children who have acute sinusitis, but a new study suggests that the drugs do not help most of them. Writing in the current issue of Pediatrics, researchers said that in most cases, the children got better on their own within three weeks. So doctors should hold off giving antibiotics, the authors say. (250 words)  _ While parents have become much more likely to place their children into car seats, they still have much to learn about the best ways to safeguard young passengers, a study by the National Highway and Transportation Safety Administration reports.  _ Ecstasy, the popular drug often associated with the club scene, may harm the memories of people who use it regularly, researchers are reporting in the journal Neurology. It's just one in a series of reports on the drug. (295 words)  _ Women who gain too much weight during pregnancy, already known to be at risk of a number of complications, may also be at risk for a post-pregnancy one: breast-feeding problems. (275 words)  _ Men don't talk about their eating disorders, it seems. They still have them, a new study reports. They're just much less likely to seek treatment for them. The findings are contained in a report in the current American Journal of Psychiatry. (195 words)  SCI-Q&AMP;A (Undated) _ A science question answered: How do those 3-D pictures work, the ones for which you focus your eyes to see hidden images? By C. Claiborne Ray. NC-POTTERY-MANIA (Charlotte, N.C.) _ North Carolina has at least 500 full-time potters, more per capita than any other state.
