Carrie, unlike Bjork, never wrapped herself in a giant swan, but her clothes seemed to reach for a level of eccentricity not lavishly appreciated outside the context of Japanese fashion magazines.  In the course of one hour on Sunday night, Carrie, the dogged romantic and sample-sale fiend played by Sarah Jessica Parker, wore, at various turns, a vintage nurse's cape, bustier and fingerless white gloves that made her look like a deranged nanny; a Prada headband that looked as if it were on its way to morphing into a Davy Crockett cap; and what appeared to be a muslin bra over a slip dress, which left the impression that Carrie had dressed herself with the Sunday morning leavings from the floor of a frat house.  ``We kind of work bit by bit,'' Patricia Field, a costume designer on ``Sex and the City,'' explained. ``We have certain ideas we might want to try, but we don't have a grand master plan.''  A few of the looks selected by Field _ a dress in an upholstery pattern and a sweater depicting a deer roaming in a forest _ came from the French designer Jean-Charles de Castelbajac, whom Field visited in Paris earlier this year when she was attending the couture shows.  What inspired Field to put Carrie in knee-high argyle socks paired with high-heel sandals _ a look that never quite imparts the idea that the wearer is a functioning, job-holding person with her own ATM card _ is harder to say.  ``We just thought, `Oh, this looks cute,''' Field said. ``It was supposed to be cold out or something like that.''  One imagines that it wasn't supposed to be too cold at the charity fashion show that Carrie participated in during the second episode. Asked to model a Dolce &AMP; Gabbana gown, Carrie, at the behest of a nutty stylist played by Alan Cumming, wound up walking down the runway in jewel-encrusted briefs and a thigh-high silk evening coat (and, winningly, in last season's Versace hair). This was the closest she got to authenticity _ or, rather, to looking like Carrie's creator, Candace Bushnell.  A WIDE CHOICE OF ADROVERS  Editors, it appears, are not the only ones in the fashion community to have quickly outgrown their infatuation with a certain proclaimed genius. Retailers may have, too.  A few weeks ago, a visitor to Loehmann's, the discount clothing emporium on lower Seventh Avenue, came upon racks of clothes, estimated in the hundreds of pieces, belonging not to the Calvin Klein, DKNY or Byblos collections of three seasons ago _ typical fare at the Manhattan store _ but rather to American fashion's most recently anointed superstar, Miguel Adrover. The clothes came from Adrover's spring 2001 collection, inspired by the world of the military and lauded by critics. Jackets at stores like Barneys were originally priced at $1,500.  ``Unfortunately, it is in the nature of our business that you are never able to predict your cuttings exactly,'' Gary Nelson, head of sales for Miguel Adrover, said, referring to the risks inherent in producing clothing lines. ``I don't know a single designer in the world who hasn't had excess inventory.''  Nelson added that Adrover's clothes are in eight Loehmann's stores and that some of those have about 20 pieces each. He also attributed the overstock to the line's rapid expansion. ``Our distribution grew dramatically from fall to spring,'' he said. ``We literally expanded globally.''  THE FASHION COUNCIL CHOOSES  Today, the 26-member board of the Council of Fashion Designers of America, a group claiming Diane Von Furstenberg, Calvin Klein and Carolina Herrera as members, will convene to select an executive director, and the man expected to be named is not a son of their own.  Stan Herman, the president of the council, said that the only candidate being considered is Peter Arnold, a 43-year-old lawyer at the firm of Brown &AMP; Wood.  If confirmed, Wood will replace Fern Mallis, the council's current executive director, who plans to join IMG, the talent agency, which will officially take over the Seventh on Sixth fashion shows. Relieved of the responsibility of producing that event, the council will focus more on fund-raising.  Arnold would not comment on the prospect of becoming the council's new director. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20010605.0207 type=story > <HEADLINE> TURNING TO SINGING AND YOGA FOR BALANCE </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-KIPLINGER-NEWSLETTER-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200106.gz"

AFGHAN-MILITARY (Washington) _ Ledeall: U.S. Special Operations Forces have been shooting at enemy forces in southern Afghanistan, in the most forceful ground combat operations by American soldiers to date, Secretary of Defense Donald Rumsfeld said Friday. Rumsfeld's remarks indicated an expanding, and more dangerous, role for American ground forces as the military heightens its efforts to find and destroy the leadership of al-Qaida and the Taliban. In a separate action against the al-Qaida network, one of its most senior members, Muhammed Atef, was killed by U.S. bombs in an airstrike near Kabul earlier this week, Rumsfeld said. By Thom Shanker, James Dao and Eric Schmitt.  With photo, NYT11.  TERROR-NETWORK (Washington) _ The reported death in Afghanistan of Mohammed Atef, al-Qaida's chief of military operations, would be a significant blow to Osama bin Laden's efforts to regain the offensive against the United States, and could mark a turning point in the Bush administration's global efforts to disrupt and destroy al-Qaida's terrorist capabilities. By James Risen.  KABUL-HOUSES (Kabul, Afghanistan) _ A flight simulator computer program, a list of flight schools in the United States and documents describing chemical, biological and nuclear warfare were found Friday in safe houses used by members of Osama Bin Laden's al-Qaida network here, among the debris left behind by al-Qaida members when they fled Kabul on Monday. By David Rohde.  With photo.  AIRLINES-CONGRESS (Washington) _ Congress gave quick approval Friday to an aviation security bill that requires the federal government to hire 28,000 employees to screen travelers and baggage at the nation's airports within one year. The Senate, by prearrangement of its members, passed the measure by unanimous voice vote late Friday morning. The House approved it by a vote of 410-9 about three hours later. By Robert Pear.  EDS: An early version of this story has moved and will be updated.  NY-FIRE-FIGHTERS (New York) _ It started with anger and an ugly protest over a decision to reduce the number of fire fighters assigned to work at the World Trade Center site. But on Friday, the sad story of the fire department at war with the mayor appeared to end. The number of fire fighters at ground zero has been restored to what it was; not, it appears, because of the threats of unions, the negotiating power of the mayor's aides or the recommendations of safety experts. Rather, the deciding factor was the singular story of an unassuming retired firefighter whose son remains buried in the rubble. By Jennifer steinhauer.  WARNACO-WACHNER (Undated) _ Linda Wachner, once the highest-paid woman executives, was forced to resign without severance as chief executive of Warnaco Group Inc. on Friday. In her 14 years as the head of Warnaco, Wachner transformed a medium-sized bra manufacturer into a $2.25 billion-a-year company with retail brands like Calvin Klein Jeans and underwear, Chaps by Ralph Lauren sportswear and Speedo swimsuits. By Leslie Kaufman.  (Editors: All clients receive all budgets, but only full-service clients receive all stories. For the latest photos and graphics from The New York Times, go to www.nytimages.com.  ATTACK-OVERVIEW (Undated) _ A synoptic look at the day's news related to the situation in Afghanistan and terrorist attacks in the United States. By Clyde Haberman. (700 words)  PAGE ONE  AFGHAN-MILITARY (Washington) _ U.S. Special Operations Forces have been shooting at enemy forces in southern Afghanistan, in the most forceful ground combat operations by American soldiers to date, Secretary of Defense Donald Rumsfeld said Friday. Rumsfeld's remarks indicated an expanding, and more dangerous, role for American ground forces as the military heightens its efforts to find and destroy the leadership of al-Qaida and the Taliban. In a separate action against the al-Qaida terrorist network of Osama bin Laden, one of its most senior members, Muhammed Atef, was killed by U.S. bombs in an airstrike near Kabul earlier this week, Rumsfeld said. By Thom Shanker, James Dao and Eric Schmitt.  With photo, NYT11.  TERROR-NETWORK (Washington) _ The reported death in Afghanistan of Mohammed Atef, al-Qaida's chief of military operations, would be a significant blow to Osama bin Laden's efforts to regain the offensive against the United States, and could mark a turning point in the Bush administration's global efforts to disrupt and destroy al-Qaida's terrorist capabilities. by James Risen.  KABUL-HOUSES (Kabul, Afghanistan) _ A flight simulator computer program, a list of flight schools in the United States and documents describing chemical, biological and nuclear warfare were found Friday in safe houses used by members of Osama Bin Laden's al-Qaida network here, among the debris left behind by al-Qaida members when they fled Kabul on Monday. By David Rohde.  With photo.  AIRLINES-CONGRESS (Washington) _ Congress gave quick approval Friday to an aviation security bill that requires the federal government to hire 28,000 employees to screen travelers and baggage at the nation's airports within one year. The Senate, by prearrangement of its members, passed the measure by unanimous voice vote late Friday morning. The House approved it by a vote of 410-9 about three hours later. By Robert Pear.  NY-FIRE-FIGHTERS (New York) _ It started with anger and an ugly protest over a decision to reduce the number of fire fighters assigned to work at the World Trade Center site. But on Friday, the sad story of the fire department at war with the mayor appeared to end. The number of fire fighters at ground zero has been restored to what it was; not, it appears, because of the threats of unions, the negotiating power of the mayor's aides or the recommendations of safety experts. Rather, the deciding factor was the singular story of an unassuming retired firefighter whose son remains buried in the rubble. By Jennifer steinhauer.  OTHER STORIES  International  AFGHAN-KANDAHAR (Quetta, Pakistan) _ The Taliban leader, Mullah Omar, one of the main targets of the U.S. war in Afghanistan, has decided to surrender the political stronghold of Kandahar within 24 hours and retreat to the mountains, the Afghan Islamic Press reported Friday. The Mullah agreed to cede control of the city to two well-known commanders from the Pashtun ethnic group, the news agency said, possibly ending the seven year reign of the extremist Islamic group that gave safe harbor to Osama bin Laden and the al-Qaida network. By Jane Perlez.  AFGHAN-HERAT (Herat, Afghanistan) _ On Tuesday, Ismail Khan, the commander who liberated this far west capital from Soviet occupation, returned triumphantly here having helped liberate it once again, this time from Taliban rule. By Friday he had been ``elected'' as governor general _ by tens of thousands of supporters who gathered at a mosque _ and with that authority he held a press conference in a villa's garden here and delivered a message to the West: thank you for your help, such as it was, but it is no longer needed. By Amy Waldman.  With photos.  AID-WORKERS (Islamabad, Pakistan) _ The tale of captivity by the Taliban, as told by the two American women. By Douglas Frantz.  With photos, NYT4-7.  AFGHAN-KUNDUZ (Kunduz, Afghanistan) _ The standoff continues in Taliban-held Kunduz; other developments, including behind-the-scenes efforts to end the stalemate. By Dexter Filkins.  With photos, NYT1-3.  National  TERROR-DEARBORN (Dearborn, Mich.) _ Half the 5,000 young Middle Eastern men on the Justice Department's list of people to question live in Dearborn, and local police have been told that they will have to help the FBI carry out its interviews. And Daniel Saab is dreading the ordeal. Not because he is on the list, but because he might have to ask the hard questions. As one of a handful of Arab-Americans on the 220-person police force in Dearborn, Saab already feels squeezed. By Jodi Wilgoren.  ATLANTA-AIRPORT (Atlanta) _ Authorities halted flights at Hartsfield Atlanta International Airport and evacuated up to 10,000 people from the terminal Friday after a man ran through a security checkpoint and eluded police. The security breach all but shut down the nation's busiest airport, its effects rippling across the United States as all flights into or out of Atlanta were delayed on the busy travel weekend before Thanksgiving. (Summary from wires) By David Firestone.  AIRPORTS-REACT (Seattle) _ Gauging the reaction of airport security workers to the federalization plan. (With feeds from Boston, Atlanta, Austin, among others.) By Sam Howe Verhovek.  TERROR-MISSING (Undated) _ The daily feature: A glimpse at the lives of people missing in the World Trade Center attacks.  Commentary (``k'' code)  Bill Keller's ``Correspondent'' and Anthony Lewis' ``Abroad at Home.'' "114057"	"it"	"appears"	" Now and then an author surprises me. Thomas H. Cook is a writer I would buy in order to read, and as with Hillerman's autobiography, he seldom disappoints, but ``The Interrogation'' (Bantam Doubleday Dell, $23.95) is disappointing. Cook's intent is intriguing: to recount an interrogation of a homeless man who is believed by the police to have kidnapped and killed a young girl. The time is 1952, so there is reference to ``an unspeakable crime,'' of which we would readily speak today. The interrogation is interesting enough, but every figure carries a heavy burden of torment, and we can guess pretty quickly that the police are on the wrong track. The novel unfolds in what is called ``real time,'' and we see the interrogators, certain they have the right man, trample on what today we would regard as the suspect's rights. "126308"	"it"	"appears"	" --Latin America: ISABEL AMORIM SICHERLE in Brazil at 55-11-30233331. </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20020924.0162 type=advis > <HEADLINE> (EDITORS: Subs to change, in 5th graf, title of Bulgakov novel from </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 09-24-02 16:55:00EDT (BC-MARTINEZ-CORRECTION-M) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Two recent events in particular define the nature of the complex relations between dictatorships and art.  Hitler's favorite filmmaker, Leni Riefensthal, celebrated her 100th birthday at the end of August. At about the same time, Martin Amis' book on Josef Stalin, ``Koba the Dread,'' a masterpiece of journalism, was published in London.  These two happenings also illuminate very clear differences between dictatorships that bend the artist into subservience, and the more stupid dictatorships that simply ignore the artist or try to silence him. Spain's Franco and most of Latin America's dictators that ruled after 1930, fall into this last category.  Everyone knows that both the Nazis and Stalin _ whose code name in the underground press was Koba _ tried to establish an art that glorified heroes and patriotism in a simple language. Several artists managed to create, even within those limits, something original or worthy.  The Soviet Union produced some formidable artists: filmmakers, like Sergei Mikhailovich Einsenstein (``Potemkin,'' ``Ivan the Terrible'') and Vsevolod Pudovkin (``Mother''); or writers like Maxim Gorki and Mikhail Bulgakov, who wrote an admirable novel, ``The Master and Margarita,'' and a really mediocre play, ``The Days of the Turbines,'' which Stalin saw 15 times.  Amis describes with devastating effectiveness how these artists were all bowed into submission, tormented and reduced to echoing the official party-line.  Hitler had at his disposal artists and thinkers equally as renowned as the Soviets. Among musicians, he had the cowed composer Richard Strauss and the accommodating conductor Wilhelm Furtwangler; among the thinkers, there was the astute Martin Heidegger and the genius militarist, Ernst Junger. But in German cinema, nobody got as far as Leni Riefenstahl.  Amis' book attempts to explain the strange indifference of many Western intellectuals to Stalin's savage crimes, which everyone knew about. But Amis goes further than that. He reveals how the dictator was completely intoxicated with power and describes in detail the intricacies of Stalin's methods of submission.  ``Bolshevism was an export product,'' Amis writes in one memorable paragraph, ``and produced almost identical results everywhere. Nazism, on the other hand, could not be duplicated. But, compared with those two, the other Fascist states were just amateurs.''  Like Nero or the first emperor of China, these dictators were actually frustrated artists, which explains why they _ in contrast to Spain's Franco, Argentina's Videla and Chile's Pinochet _ were interested in having intellectuals at their side. With tongue-in cheek, Amis suggests that one could put together an interesting book with the poems of Ho Chi Minh, Mao, Stalin and Fidel Castro _ illustrated with watercolors by Adolf Hitler.  The West's intellectuals who defended Stalin, based their arguments on moral grounds. To build a better world for the long term, it was necessary to tolerate, meanwhile, a worse world in the short term. The end justified any means.  The Communist ghost conjured up by Sen. Joseph McCarthy in the late 1940s _ and revived 30 years later by his pathetic disciples in the Southern Cone _ was based on the same principle. Faced with the menace of a Third World War, you had to clean up the dissidents.  The long-lived Leni Riefenstahl is even more audacious. She has found an aesthetic justification for her Nazi propaganda films which, by the way, are probably the best ever made. To celebrate her centennial, she agreed to be interviewed in Poking, the town where she lives, on the Austrian border.  Millions of people have seen her outstanding work, ``Triumph of the Will,'' and know what it is about. A glorification of Hitler and of Arian beauty, it was filmed in 1934 during the Nazi Party Congress in Nuremberg.  Its message is defined in the opening scene: a sky full of bright fluffy clouds, from which descends, like some sort of apparition, the Fuhrer's plane. All of the Third Reich's resources were placed at Riefenstahl's service. She made use of 30 cameras, an endless assortment of lenses and 120 assistants.  ``I wasn't in the least interested in politics,'' said Riefenstahl, ingenuously. ``Hitler interested me only in that he was offering jobs to 6 million unemployed Germans.''  Leni insists that her documentaries only record fact, not ideology, and that neither ``Triumph of the Will'' nor ``Olympia,'' the film she made of the 1936 Berlin Olympic Games, has a single anti-Jewish comment.  Several journalist reminded her that although her films may register ``facts,'' her own declarations later contradicted that. In 1938, she insisted she knew nothing about Kristal Nacht _ which marked the beginning of the Nazi persecution of the Jews _ in spite of the fact that she had actually filmed scenes of the outrage. And in 1944 she went on to say, ``It's not important if it's a concentration camp, as long as it produces beauty.''  The dictatorships of Franco (Spain), Trujillo (Dominican Republic), Geisel (Brazil) and Viola and Videla (Argentina) generated only one valuable art form: that expressing resistance or desperation. Their propaganda creations, such as the Valley of the Fallen or the glorifications of World Cup football are mediocre, valueless and merely satisfy illiterate vanities. It is doubtful they will leave a mark on anyone's memory.  Martin Amis' chronicle and Leni Riefenstahl's legacy prove, however, a strange fact: Some dictators may disappear into opprobrium, as Hitler and Stalin did, but their curiosity about creative talent, their desire to tame it and use for their own ends, managed to leave behind malignant jewels, like ``Triumph of the Will,'' or tormented ones, like ``Ivan the Terrible.''  The history of Evil, it appears, treads countless paths _ but not all of them lead to the same Hell.  (Tomas Eloy Martinez is the author of ``The Peron Novel,'' ``Santa Evita,'' and ``The Flight of the Queen'' which recently won Spain's most prestigious award for fiction. His books have been translated into more than 30 languages. He is director of Latin American Studies at Rutgers University and travels widely as a writer and journalist.) Our report has also moved to Newscom and via AP PhotoExpress.  The following photos have been sent as part of the New York Times photo package for Wednesday, Sept. 25, 2002.  (NYT37) NEW HAVEN, Conn. -- Sept. 25, 2002 -- YALE-PROTEST-2 -- People sympathetic to a union protest gathered on the Yale campus Wednesday to support negotiating efforts between the university and employees. New Haven police arrested about 800 of the well-behaved Yale University students, employees, union members after they clogged a popular downtown street to protest the university's relations with its workers. Unlike epic protests of yore, however, this was no pitched battle between angry union members and police battalions in riot gear. Union officials said that the 800 arrested were the largest contingent to be arrested in New Haven since the civil rights marches of the 1960's. (Ozier Muhammad/The New York Times)  (NYT36) NEW YORK -- Sept. 25, 2002 -- NY-WTC-SUNFLOWERS -- Sunflowers are the ugly ducklings of the plant world, with fuzzy weedlike stems and leaves that give no clue of the golden bloom to come. But they grow fast, and to towering heights, which is why the artist Xavier Roux chose them as his medium for a tribute to the World Trade Center, ``a giant, walking sculpture'' in his words. In early summer, Roux planted 4,000 of them, from ground zero to Chelsea, in the shape of the twin towers. Roux tends to the sunflowers at the corner of Greenwich Avenue and Seventh Avenue on Wednesday September 25, 2002. (Chang W. Lee/The New York Times)  (NYT35) NEW HAVEN, Conn. -- Sept. 25, 2002 -- YALE-PROTEST -- People sympathetic to a union protest gathered on the Yale campus Wednesday to support negotiating efforts between the university and employees. The suit is an effort by state Attorney General Jim Ryan to block Gov. George H. Ryan from conducting an unprecedented series of clemency hearings next month for everyone on death row. Gov. Ryan has signaled that the hearings may spare the lives of many, or even all, of the 158 condemned prisoners. Confusion over the two Ryans, who are not related, leave the GOP in danger of losing a statehouse it has occupied for 26 years. A poster for Attorney General Jim Ryan's gubernatorial campaign at his office in Chicago on Wednesday, September 25, 2002. Mahamid's effort to stop a Palestinian suicide bombing aboard a Tel Aviv bus last week captured the dilemma of Israel's vexed Arab minority. For Arabs, citizenship in Israel can be a tormenting affair. Rami took it for granted that his fellow Israeli Arabs would approve of his act, but his father, Mahmoud Mahamid, is less certain, though his family does not want to break that to the wounded youth. The certificate praised Mahamid for ``saving life with great courage and initiative'' and celebrated his ``good citizenship.'' She is known for her designs for contemporary Tibetan rugs and she is now among the biggest importers of Tibetan carpets to the United States. Her career has been spent helping Third World artisans make beautiful things. Odegard, 53, does it because she believes that some day the making of handicrafts may resuscitate the society that is disintegrating around the craftsmen. Torricelli refused to answer questions Wednesday about his efforts to keep private a memo detailing an ethics investigation against him, saying his court motion ``speaks for itself.'' Javits Convention Center in July at which 4,000 New Yorkers gathered and decried the original six plans for rebuilding the World Trade Center has been cited as an exercise in the very principles of participatory democracy, in which informed public discussion leads to the best decisions. Less chronicled is the experience of 800 people who could not make it to the Javits Center that day but instead convened over the Internet over a two-week period to discuss many of the same questions, at more length and perhaps with more nuance. From left, members of a discussion group on ground zero's future: Maria Grieco, Debra Lott, Margaret Duffy, Ellen Datlow, Vincent Pecoraro and Bill Love, gathered near ground zero. Slightly larger than an ID badge, the Vocera unit clips onto employees' clothing. Its operation is largely hands-free. A large button on the front turns the unit on. The user then says the name of a co-worker, and voice recognition software makes the connection through a standard 802.11b, or Wi-Fi, network and a special server. (The New York Times)  (NYT7) UNDATED -- Sept. 25, 2002 -- CIR-GEEK-NOTES -- When connecting digital devices -- like camcorders or the new Apple iPod for Windows -- to a PC, a high-speed port works best. There are two relatively new standards for high-speed file transfer, FireWire (technically, IEEE 1394) and USB 2.0. The card can be installed in any available PCI expansion slot and creates two ports for each type of connection. Keyspan supplies software to configure the card to a Mac or a PC. The unit sells for $99 at computer and electronic stores and online at www.keyspan.com. TOP  Lede story:  CAMPAIGN-SENATE (Washington) _ The political disarray caused by the death of Sen. Paul Wellstone of Minnesota put a sharp new focus on the battle for control of the Senate, leaving grim strategists in both parties scrambling for their footing Saturday in a national struggle that remains too close to call little more than a week before Election Day. By Alison Mitchell.  (The story is scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  Top center story:  RUSSIA-HOSTAGES (Moscow) _ Russian troops violently crushed a 57-hour hostage siege in Moscow early Saturday, handing President Vladimir V. Putin a high-risk triumph over a terrorist threat. But the price, the loss of more than 60 civilian lives, could yet prove dear. Still, almost 700 people were freed from two days' captivity in the dramatic pre-dawn rescue, a whirlwind of explosions, gunfire and clouds of disabling gas by perhaps 250 anti-terrorist soldiers and police. By Michael Wines.  With photos NYT 1-10, graphic and map.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``i'' news file.)  Off-lead story:  SNIPER-RECONSTRUCT (Washington) _ A detailed reconstruction of the sniper investigation: Charles A. Moose, the police chief in Montgomery County, Md., who led the investigation, said that police work took persistence, teamwork and community support _ but almost always depended on chance a well. ``As police chief, I know you need a lot of luck,'' Moose said. ``I'd like to think it's all skill and moxie and brains. But it's mostly luck.'' By David Johnston and Don Van Natta Jr.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  FOLD  SNIPER-MALVO (Undated) _ John Lee Malvo spent much of his life in search of a father figure. In John Allen Muhammad, the teenager may have finally found the role model and mentor he had longed for. And in the end, it appears, as the facts of the boy's unsettled existence emerge, sustaining Muhammad's acceptance may have become more important to Malvo than the lives of the 13 innocent victims that police now believe the teenager helped to gun down. By Lynette Clemetson.  With photo NYT 23.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  BOTTOM  ISRAEL-BUSES (Jerusalem) _ ``You know the difference between Russian roulette and Israeli roulette?'' asked Capt. Dan Ravitz. ``In Russian roulette, you choose your bullet. Here, you just pick a bus.'' By James Bennet.  With photos NYT 11-17.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``i'' news file.)  IRAQ-FRANCE (Paris) _ In a bold diplomatic challenge to the United States, France announced Saturday that it may formally introduce its own resolution on disarming Iraq at the U.N. Security Council if that was the only way to reach agreement. By Elaine Sciolino.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``i'' news file.)  INSIDE STORIES PROMOTED ON PAGE 1:  SNIPER-RELIGION (Washington) _ By many accounts, religion had been a stabilizing factor in the life of John Allen Muhammad, who is charged with murder in the sniper rampage that killed 10 people in the Washington region. But as the behavior of the suspected sniper went out of control this month, so did the character of the religious utterances. By Blaine Harden.  With photos NYT 23 and 24.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  SNIPER-MOOSE (Rockville, Md.) _ For Charles A. Moose, the police chief of Montgomery County, Md., the intensely public manhunt posed extraordinary challenges. Suddenly, a man whose department handled just 19 homicides last year was commanding a task force of more than 1,000 state and federal investigators, under the unforgiving glare of hundreds of reporters, all the while dancing a delicate and dangerous minuet with his quarry. By Sheryl Gay Stolberg.  With photos NYT 18 and 19.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  SNIPER-VICTIM-FUNERAL (Silver Spring, Md.) _ The buses started lining up here Saturday in the early morning darkness. They and their drivers from across Maryland, Virginia and Washington were here for a procession and funeral for the last known victim of the sniper shootings, Conard E. Johnson, a driver for Ride On, the transit agency in Montgomery County, Md. By Jayson Blair.  With photos NYT 20-22.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  HAWAIIANS-LAS-VEGAS (Las Vegas) _ A broad swath of the Hawaiian middle class _ hotel workers, retired police officers, teachers and, increasingly, nurses and other professionals _ has abandoned its economically depressed home state in the last 10 years, seeking better jobs, cheaper houses and easier lives here. This stream of migrants has merged with a deluge of visitors _ Hawaiians make 300,000 trips here every year _ to transform Las Vegas into perhaps the most Hawaiian city on the mainland. By Lawrence Downes.  With photos NYT 25 and 26.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.) The lineup is fluid, depending on developments. Presently planned are:  IRAQ-ELECT (Baghdad, Iraq) -- Ledeall on the Iraqi election. By Dexter Filkins.  IRAQ-ELECT-BAGHDAD (Baghdad, Iraq) -- The scene in Baghdad on election day. By Dexter Filkins.  IRAQ-ELECT-SCENE (Baghdad, Iraq) -- The scene on the streets of Baghdad. By John F. Burns.  IRAQ-ELECT-ASSESS (Baghdad, Iraq) -- News analysis of the Iraq election, particularly as it related to the insurgency. By John F. Burns.  IRAQ-ELECT-MOSUL (Mosul, Iraq) -- The scene in Mosul: Kurds turn out to vote but find no voting materials. By Christine Hauser.  IRAQ-ELECT-NAJAF (Najaf, Iraq) -- The scene in Najaf. By Edward Wong.  IRAQ-ELECT-BUSH (Washington) -- President Bush speaks about the election, claiming a victory. By David E. Sanger.  IRAQ-ELECT-PRESS (Amman, Jordan) -- The reaction of the Arab press. By Hassan M. Fattah.  MIDEAST (Jerusalem) -- Israel is prepared to hand over security control of several West Bank cities to the Palestinians in the next few days, Defense Minister Shaul Mofaz said on Sunday, in the latest sign of increasing cooperation between the two sides. By Greg Myre.  IRAQ-AUDIT (Undated) -- An audit shows irregularities in spending on Iraq. By Erik Eckholm.  IRAQ-U.N.-OIL (Undated) -- Developments in the oil-for-food case. By Julia Preston and Judith Miller.  CHINA-MISSING-GIRLS (Anxi, China) -- For farming families in the lush mountains of coastal Fujian Province, the famous crop is oolong tea and the favorite source of labor is sons. There is such a glut of boys here -- roughly 134 are born for every 100 girls -- that the imbalance has forced an unlikely response from the Chinese government. To convince more families to have girls, it has decided in some cases to pay families that already have daughters. By Jim Yardley.  CANADA-GAY-MARRIAGE (Toronto) -- The Roman Catholic Church has opened a major campaign to defeat a bill to legalize same-sex marriage across the country, which the government says will be introduced in early February when a deeply divided Parliament reconvenes. By Clifford Krauss.  BRAZIL-MILITARY-ABUSES (Rio de Janeiro, Brazil) -- Incriminating documents supposedly incinerated years ago by the government have suddenly reappeared. As a result, Brazil is being forced to confront one of the most distasteful aspects of its past: the death, disappearance or torture of political prisoners during 21 years of military dictatorship. By Larry Rohter.  NETHERLANDS-MUSLIMS (Paris) -- Can angry young Muslims dictate what is and is not acceptable in the traditionally open-minded world of Dutch arts? In the past few weeks, it appears, the answer has been yes. By Marlise Simons.  TURKEY-POLITICS (Istanbul, Turkey) -- After a raucous session of name-calling and a chair-throwing melee that had to be broken up by riot police, the country's main opposition leader beat back a challenge by reformers on Sunday and was re-elected as the leader of the Republican People's Party. By Susan Sachs.  IRELAND-ROADS (Tara, Ireland) -- Ancient England may have Stonehenge, but ancient Ireland has the Hill of Tara. The 6,000-year-old sacred site in the middle of quiet rolling fields is revered here as the burial place of 140 kings, and as the formative birthplace of this land's national identity. Modern Ireland also has Dublin, whose burgeoning metropolitan area is home to about 1.5 million people out of Ireland's population of close to 4 million. The city's growing sprawl is causing a clash that is affecting the entire country, as lovers of the mythical and prehistoric Ireland try to preserve the placidity of Tara against the needs of local residents struggling to commute to the capital on antiquated and inadequate roads. By Brian Lavery.  IRAQ-COPTER-VICTIMS (Kailua, Hawaii) -- Portraits of the 37 American troops who were killed in Iraq on Wedsday, the deadliest day for Americans since the United States invaded Iraq 22 months ago. By Dean E. Murphy and Sarah Kershaw.  MEDICARE-DRUGS (Washington) -- Rules under which employers will be allowed to collect federal subsides for prescription drug benefits for retirees. By Robert Pear.  ANTIABORTION-TACTIC (Undated) -- Abortion opponents have have long fought on two fronts, seeking laws to restrict access to the procedure and running pregnancy centers to convince women with unplanned pregnancies and convince them to carry to term, one by one. Now, the centers say they have discovered a powerful new tool: ultrasounds. Over the last 18 months, Christian anti-abortion activists on the national and local level have started major fund-raising campaigns to outfit centers with ultrasound equipment. By Neela Banerjee.  JACKSON-TRIAL (Santa Maria, Calif.) -- Fourteen months after a small army of sheriff's deputies laid siege to Michael Jackson's Neverland ranch seeking evidence of the entertainer's suspected lewd acts with a 13-year-old cancer patient, California's latest and greatest celebrity show trial is set to begin Monday with the first stages of jury selection. By John M. Broder.  NUKE-WASTE (Undated) -- Nuclear energy advocates are backing away from the assertion that the proposed Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository would have to open before anyone could order a new power reactor, as completion looks later and less certain, and the prospect for new reactors improves. By Matthew L. Wald.  DEMS-PREBUTTLE (Washington) -- With President Bush scheduled to deliver his State of the Union address on Wednesday, Democratic leaders in Congress are planning a pre-emptive strike of their own on Monday, challenging Bush on his policy in Iraq and plans for Social Security. In a prebuttal to the presidential address, Sen. Harry Reid of Nevada is set to urge the president to use Sunday's elections as an opportunity to change course in Iraq and come clean with the American public by presenting a coherent plan for what lies ahead in Iraq. By Carl Hulse.  KERRY (Washington) -- Sen. John Kerry appears on NBC's Meet the Press. By Adam Nagourney.  WHITEHOUSE-LETTER (Washington) -- The White House letter. By Elisabeth Bumiller. IBM-LENOVO (Undated) -- On its face, IBM's planned sale of its personal computer business to Lenovo of China for $1.75 billion hardly seems a deal that would prompt a national security investigation by the Bush administration. Most IBM personal computers are now produced in China. Like other PCs, the IBM machines are powered by Intel microprocessors and are assembled with chips and parts made around the world, though mainly in East Asia. But the Committee on Foreign Investment in the United States, a multiagency group, will carry out a formal investigation, which is expected to last 45 days, people close to the inquiry have confirmed. By Simon Romero.  DISNEY-BOOK-MEDIA (Orlando, Fla.) -- Analysts and investors gathering at the Walt Disney World resort here on Monday for Walt Disney Co.'s annual conference will be buzzing about DisneyWar: The Battle for the Magic Kingdom, a new book written by James B. Stewart and said to have sections highly critical of Disney's outgoing chief executive, Michael D. Eisner. By Laura M. Holson and Lorne Manley.  MEDIA-TALK (Undated) -- A new magazine is marketed to plastic surgery patients. Guess who advertises?; Stan Lee's former collaborators react to his windfall settlement with Marvel; In the latest example of a Hollywood trend, Kevin Connolly is trying to dump his manager -- and his 10 percent commission -- just as he makes it big.  HURLEYBURLY (Undated) -- The 1998 film version of David Rabe's Hurlyburly may be one of the all-time bad movies made from an all-time good play. But that misbegotten film now serves an unexpected purpose. Updated from Rabe's 1984 work and chock-full of cell phones, the movie highlights how shrewd it was for the exhilarating new off-Broadway production to keep the play where it started: in the cocaine-fueled Hollywood of the '80s. By Caryn James.  WENDY-DONIGER (Undated) -- Wendy Doniger is one of the foremost scholars of Hindu mythology, the author, editor or translator of 20 books, and a professor with multiple appointments at the University of Chicago, where she has taught since 1978. By Edward Rothstein.  EASTWOOD-CRITICISM (Los Angeles) -- When the Clint Eastwood film Million Dollar Baby first came out, critics praised the film for its subtle power, moving performances and the quiet confidence of its director. But not wanting to give away its ending, few mentioned that a controversial social issue was buried in its plot. Now that it has been nominated for seven Oscars, social activists and conservative commentators have emerged to criticize the film, which they say sends a message advocating assisted suicide. By Sharon Waxman.  SUNDANCE-NOTEBOOK (Park City, Utah) -- A Critic's Notebook wrapping up the Sundance Festival. By A.O. Scott.  SHAKESPEARE-MILITARY (Queenstown, Md.) -- For the last two years, the U.S. Air Force has contracted with Movers &amp; Shakespeares, a company run by Kenneth L. Adelman, a Republican political consultant who is probably best known as having been President Ronald Reagan's chief adviser on arms control. Adelman and his wife, Carol, also a Reagan administration veteran, are amateur Shakespeare scholars and Shakespeare lovers, and they have marshaled their avocation and their high-level contacts into a management-training business. By Bruce Weber.  CD-REVIEW (Undated) -- The weekly feature. By Jon Pareles.  KAFKA-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) -- Janet Maslin reviews Kafka on the Shore, by Haruki Murakami (Knopf). CHINA-ARTS (Washington) -- As it races toward a more market-driven economy, China is building new theaters, concert halls and museums nearly as fast as it is erecting Western-style shopping malls, hotels and business complexes. But after a half century of the arts being supported exclusively by the government, many arts organizations are on their own to develop financing, programming and audiences. A cadre of nascent Chinese arts executives spent the past two weeks at the Kennedy Center, getting an intense immersion in how the nonprofit arts operate. By Lynette Clemetson.  SMITHS-MUSICAL-REVIEW (London) -- The Smiths -- a band from Manchester, England, whose singer and lyricist, Morrissey, taught a generation-wide cult how to mope with melodramatic self-consciousness -- are getting a West End treatment in Some Girls Are Bigger Than Others, a music-theater piece based on Smiths songs. The Smiths' lyrics were proudly defenseless and unguarded, yet the staging doesn't wrap them in obvious scenarios. The show is an allusive, surreal, ever-mutating fantasia on love and sex, family and control, violence and death. By Jon Pareles.  MCCARTHY-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) -- No Country for Old Men, by Cormac McCarthy. Reviewed by Michiko Kakutani.  SOMERS-THEATER-REVIEW (New York) -- The Blonde in the Thunderbird, a solo show starring Suzanne Somers. Reviewed by Charles Isherwood.  CD-REVIEWS (Undated) -- New hip-hop CDs from Slim Thug, Webbie and the Diplomats. Reviewed by Kelefa Sanneh.  FRANCE-OPERA-FEST (Aix-en-Provence, France) -- Before this month's Aix-en-Provence Festival, the buzz centered on Patrice Chereau's Cosi Fan Tutte, his first venture into opera in 11 years. Mozart's works have been the focus of this festival since it was founded in the late 1940s, and usually two of his operas are presented each season in the open-air courtyard of the former Archbishop's Palace here. This year, the other is La Clemenza di Tito, directed by Lukas Hemleb, with Paul Daniel conducting the Mahler Chamber Orchestra. By Alan Riding.  ARTS-BRIEFLY (Undated) -- News and notes from the world of arts and entertainment.  ONLINE-MAPS (San Francisco) -- In the last few weeks, both Google and Yahoo published documentation making it significantly easier for programmers to link virtually any kind of Internet data to Web-based maps and, in Google's case, satellite imagery. Later this summer, Microsoft plans to introduce a competing service, Virtual Earth, with software that programmers will be able to use in similarly creative ways. So far the uses have been noncommercial. But Yahoo, Google and Microsoft are creating the services with the expectation that they will become a focal point in one of the next significant growth areas in Internet advertising: contextual advertisements tied to specific locations. By John Markoff.  HOLLYWOOD-CHRISTIANS (Los Angeles) -- Mainstream Hollywood, after decades of ignoring the pious, or occasionally defying them, is adjusting to what it perceives to be a rising religiosity in American culture. Producers, directors, studio executives and marketing specialists have been looking to either mollify or entice an audience that made its power felt with last year's Passion of the Christ. That film, along with the empowerment of a Christian conservative bloc after the last presidential election, helped change attitudes and practices in an industry usually known for its secularism. By Sharon Waxman.  MARTHA-COMEBACK (Undated) -- By attempting a corporate comeback while still serving out her felony sentence, Martha Stewart is in uncharted territory. And so, it appears, is her company's stock. While many media executives are lamenting investors' lack of enthusiasm for their companies' shares, Martha Stewart Living Omnimedia has been on a tear. Its stock price has nearly tripled in the last year, a period during which the Standard &amp; Poor's media index has gone sideways. By Richard Siklos.  CARR-COLUMN (Undated) -- Two strivers came together in a room in July of 2003. One was a former body builder turned actor who wanted to be king, at least of California, and the other was a former CPA who has refashioned himself into a media mogul. Each had something the other wanted, although it could be argued that David Pecker, the chief executive of American Media Inc. needed Arnold Schwarzenegger more. The weekly media column, by David Carr.  E-COMMERCE-COLUMN (Undated) -- After giving its Internet competitors a huge head start, Clear Channel is ready to play catch-up. The radio industry's dominant company, with more than 1,200 stations, has begun introducing its first meaningful online strategy. By Bob Tedeschi.  TERROR-FICTION (Undated) -- The author of Incendiary, a novel about a fictitious London bombing that was released July 7, goes online to ask what he should do with his book now. By Tom Zeller Jr.  POTTER-PUBLISHER (Paris) -- This is the magical summer season when Hogwarts students loll on vacation and Bloomsbury Publishing's chief executive, Nigel Newton, hunkers down in his 18th-century Soho Square offices in London to monitor sales of the sixth installment of the Harry Potter epic. By the end of this year, analysts expect that Bloomsbury will have amassed sizable resources to allow the publishing house to press onward with its global expansion and to search for new writers. By Doreen Carvajal.  DATA-AD-COLUMN (Undated) -- Over the last few years, financial services and technology companies have been quietly tweaking their advertising to incorporate themes about the safety of customers' data. But in the wake of recent crises, that message has shifted from the background to center stage. By Eric Dash.  GLF-WIE-MARKETING (Undated) -- Michelle Wie came in 23rd at the U.S. Women's Open last month, behind other personable young golfers. Wie failed to survive elimination in the PGA Tour's John Deere Classic this month and fell short in her attempt to qualify for the Masters tournament next year. STOCKS-TAX-RATES (Undated) -- Millions of investors who bought dividend-paying stocks after President Bush persuaded Congress to slash the taxes on investments are actually being taxed at rates almost 50 percent higher than what the president promised them, two new analyses show. By David Cay Johnston.  CLIMATE-CHANGE-DISPUTE (Undated) -- A rare public dispute has flared between two Republican House committee chairmen over an inquiry one of them began last month into the integrity of an influential study of global temperature trends. The study, published in 1998 and 1999, meshed data from modern thermometers and indirect evidence of past warmth or cold, such as variations in tree rings. The result was a curve with a distinctive hockey stick shape showing little variation for nearly 1,000 years and then a sharp upward hook (the blade) in recent decades. The resulting hockey stick curve has since become a familiar icon used in efforts to curtail emissions of greenhouse gases linked to global warming.  CIA-LEAK (Undated) -- Lede-all on developments in the Karl Rove affair. By David Johnston and Lorne Manly.  CIA-LEAK-LAW (Undated) -- A look at the law that is at the bottom of the Rove controversy. By Adam Liptak.  WHITEHOUSE-LETTER (Washington) --When Laura Bush said in a television interview last week that she hoped her husband would name a woman to replace the retiring Justice Sandra Day O'Connor on the Supreme Court, a lot of people saw it as a top item on the first lady's honey-do list. But Republicans close to the White House said that people had it reversed. Mrs. Bush, they said, was not so much nudging her husband as reflecting his thinking. By Elisabeth Bumiller.  TERROR-FBI (Washington) -- On an FBI terrorist surveillance program that rights advocates say threatens to clamp down on their rights to protest Bush administration policies. By Eric Lichtblau. CIR-CIRCUITS-COLUMN (Undated) -- The weekly State of the Art column: David Pogue looks at free phone-related services.  CIR-BASICS (Undated) -- Some glow at the touch. Others hum softly into the night. All of them, with their sensuous curves and luminescent faces, beg to be beheld. They are a new crop of personal computers that emphasize the personal. With attention to form, texture and materials, these machines are intended to make a statement about their owners. These are PCs meant as much to be seen as they are to be used. By Michel Marriott. CULTURE AND ENTERTAINMENT (e code)  WILSON-MUSIC-REVIEW (New York) -- Review of Brian Wilson and Pet Sounds, a 40th anniversary celebration at the Beacon Theater. By Ben Ratliff.  TIFFANY-EXHIBIT-REVIEW (New York) -- Review of the Louis Comfort Tiffany and Laurelton Hall exhibition at the Metropolitan Museum of Art. By Roberta Smith.  BEYONCE-NOTEBOOK (Undated) -- Kelefa Sanneh looks at how much has changed for Beyonce in a short time.  SUNDANCE-FILM-FEST (Undated) -- Sorting out the Sundance Film Festival schedule, to be announced on Wednesday. By David M. Halbfinger.  RUSSIA-BOOK-PRIZE (Moscow) -- To help put itself back on the world literary map, Russia is instituting a big new book prize that it is calling, helpfully, the Big Book Prize. It promises to be the second most lucrative book prize in the world, and 14 Russian authors are vying for it. The winner will be announced in a gala ceremony Wednesday evening in Moscow. By Sophia Kishkovsky.  VISITORS-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) -- Permanent Visitors by Kevin Moffett (University of Iowa Press) and Things Kept, Things Left Behind by Jim Tomlinson (University of Iowa Press). By S. Kirk Walsh.  HOUSE AND HOME (l code)  DIGITAL-HOME-SAFETY (Undated -- Home sweet home is becoming, finally, home smart home. The family group is becoming the family network: computers and appliances and services are shared and distributed through the house. Nesting has become digital nesting, as Americans bring the world home to work and play. But it is not home safe home. People know to lock their doors and close the blinds, but most, security experts agree, haven't developed the same habits in the rapidly expanding digital world at home. By William L. Hamilton.  With photo.  With:  DIGITAL-HOME-PROTECT (Undated) --Tips on protecting your home from a hacker's attack. William L. Hamilton.  JAGGER-STYLE (Atlantic, N.J.) -- In an era of music careers created in the democratic nowhere of MySpace, where the members of hot bands dress as if they were office temps, the days of the rock show as spectacle and the rock star as circus star are unquestionably numbered. Yet arena rock still has a certifiable god in Mick Jagger. And, as the Rolling Stones blew through Atlantic City last week on the last leg of its Bigger Band tour, Jagger gave a performance that was a master class in the school of rock style. By Guy Trebay.  With photo.  FASHION-FORMAL (Undated) -- Fashion, it appears, is in the throes of one of its inevitable mood swings, the years of casual, sketchily improvised party dressing giving way to a more formal look. The days of jeans and a dressy top are long gone, said Lela Rose, a young Texan whose tent dresses and pinafores seem conceived for steel magnolias. This is a much more feminine time. By Ruth La Ferla.  BOTULISM-REGULATE (Undated) -- A Florida couple who went to a doctor for Botox in 2004 and instead received injections of botulinum toxin, the deadly bacteria that cause botulism, may have received almost 3,000 times the lethal dose of the toxin, according to a report published this week in The Journal of the American Medical Association. The couple survived. The report said regulators should tighten the rules surrounding sales of this toxin to prevent it from happening again. By Natasha Singer.  WORKPLACE-BLUNDERS (Undated) -- Nancy Pelosi, speaker-elect of the House of Representatives, did something last week that plenty of working Americans have done: She made a blunder on the job. Pelosi is certainly not about to lose the job she (almost) has. Even though she is one of the country's most powerful leaders, and the politics of her workplace are on a different scale than those of most offices, her predicament has parallels to the lives of everyday executives who fumble a big opportunity. But when anyone, be it a public figure or private citizen, suffers a political setback in the workplace, can he or she ever really recover? By Stephanie Rosenbloom.  With photo.  ONLINE-SHOPPING (Undated) -- Michelle Slatalla in search of ways to organize her home office.  For information and repeats call the News Service at (888) 346-9867 or (212) 556-1927.
